class="toclevel-1 tocsection-52"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-53"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-54"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-55"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">17</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Names_of_Japan" title="Names of Japan">Names of Japan</a></div>\n<p>The Japanese word for Japan is <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">\xe6\x97\xa5\xe6\x9c\xac</span></span>, which is pronounced <i>Nihon</i> or <i>Nippon</i> and literally means "the origin of the sun". The character <i>nichi</i><span style="font-weight: normal">&#32;(<span lang="ja">\xe6\x97\xa5</span>)</span> means "sun" or "day"; <i>hon</i><span style="font-weight: normal">&#32;(<span lang="ja">\xe6\x9c\xac</span>)</span> means "base" or "origin".<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The compound therefore means "origin of the sun" and is the source of the popular Western <a href="/wiki/Epithet" title="Epithet">epithet</a> "Land of the Rising Sun".<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The earliest record of the name <i>Nihon</i> appears in the Chinese historical records of the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Old_Book_of_Tang" title="Old Book of Tang">Old Book of Tang</a></i>. At the end of the seventh century, a delegation from Japan requested that <i>Nihon</i> be used as the name of their country. This name may have its origin in a letter sent in 607 and recorded in the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Sui" title="Book of Sui">official history</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui dynasty</a>. <a href="/wiki/Prince_Sh%C5%8Dtoku" title="Prince Sh\xc5\x8dtoku">Prince Sh\xc5\x8dtoku</a>, the Regent of Japan, sent a mission to China with a letter in which he called himself "the Emperor of the Land where the Sun rises" (\xe6\x97\xa5\xe5\x87\xba\xe8\x99\x95\xe5\xa4\xa9\xe5\xad\x90). The message said: "Here, I, the emperor of the country where the sun rises, send a letter to the emperor of the country where the sun sets. How are you[?]\xe2\x80\x9d.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:King_of_Na_gold_seal_faces.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/King_of_Na_gold_seal_faces.jpg/220px-King_of_Na_gold_seal_faces.jpg" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/King_of_Na_gold_seal_faces.jpg/330px-King_of_Na_gold_seal_faces.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/King_of_Na_gold_seal_faces.jpg/440px-King_of_Na_gold_seal_faces.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4142" data-file-height="2066" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:King_of_Na_gold_seal_faces.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The "<a href="/wiki/King_of_Na_gold_seal" title="King of Na gold seal">King of Na gold seal</a>", said to have been granted to <a href="/wiki/Nakoku" title="Nakoku">Na</a> king of <a href="/wiki/Wa_(Japan)" title="Wa (Japan)">Wa (Japan)</a> by <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Guangwu_of_Han" title="Emperor Guangwu of Han">Emperor Guangwu of Han</a> in 57 CE. The seal reads "\xe6\xbc\xa2\xe5\xa7\x94\xe5\xa5\xb4\xe5\x9c\x8b\xe7\x8e\x8b". <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_National_Museum" title="Tokyo National Museum">Tokyo National Museum</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Prior to the adoption of <i>Nihon</i>, other terms such as <i><a href="/wiki/Yamato_people" title="Yamato people">Yamato</a></i><span style="font-weight: normal">&#32;(<span lang="ja">\xe5\xa4\xa7\xe5\x92\x8c</span>, or "Great Wa")</span> and <i><a href="/wiki/Wa_(Japan)" title="Wa (Japan)">Wakoku</a></i><span style="font-weight: normal">&#32;(<span lang="ja">\xe5\x80\xad\xe5\x9b\xbd</span>)</span> were used. The term <i>Wa</i><span style="font-weight: normal">&#32;(<span lang="ja">\xe5\x92\x8c</span>)</span> is a homophone of <i>Wo</i> \xe5\x80\xad (pronounced "Wa" by the Japanese), which has been used by the Chinese as a designation for the Japanese as early as the third century <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms" title="Three Kingdoms">Three Kingdoms</a> period. Another form of <i>Wa</i> (\xe5\xa7\x94, <i>Wei</i> in Chinese) was used for an early state in Japan called <a href="/wiki/Nakoku" title="Nakoku">Nakoku</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> However, the Japanese disliked some connotation of <a href="/wiki/Wa_(Japan)" title="Wa (Japan)">Wa</a> \xe5\x80\xad (which has been associated in China with concepts like "dwarf" or "pygmy"), and it was therefore replaced with the substitute character <i>Wa</i><span style="font-weight: normal">&#32;(<span lang="ja">\xe5\x92\x8c</span>)</span>, meaning "togetherness, harmony".<sup id="cite_ref-fuzita_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fuzita-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The English word Japan possibly derives from the historical <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a> pronunciation of \xe6\x97\xa5\xe6\x9c\xac. The <a href="/wiki/Old_Mandarin" title="Old Mandarin">Old Mandarin</a> or possibly early <a href="/wiki/Wu_Chinese" title="Wu Chinese">Wu Chinese</a> pronunciation of Japan was recorded by <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a> as <i>Cipangu</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> In modern <a href="/wiki/Shanghainese" title="Shanghainese">Shanghainese</a>, a Wu dialect, the pronunciation of characters <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">\xe6\x97\xa5\xe6\x9c\xac</span></span> Japan is <i>Zeppen</i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[z\xc9\x99\xca\x94p\xc9\x99n]</a></span>. The old <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">Malay</a> word for Japan, <i>Japun</i> or <i>Japang</i>, was borrowed from a southern coastal Chinese dialect, probably <a href="/wiki/Hokkien" title="Hokkien">Fukienese</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ningbo_dialect" title="Ningbo dialect">Ningpo</a><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\xe2\x80\x94and this Malay word was encountered by <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese</a> traders in Southeast Asia in the 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-ahd_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ahd-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> These <a href="/wiki/Nanban_trade" title="Nanban trade">Early Portuguese traders</a> then brought the word to <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The first record of this name in English is in a book published in 1577 and spelled <i>Giapan</i>, in a translation of a 1565 letter written by a Portuguese Jesuit <a href="/wiki/Lu%C3%ADs_Fr%C3%B3is" title="Lu\xc3\xads Fr\xc3\xb3is">Lu\xc3\xads Fr\xc3\xb3is</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From the <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Restoration" title="Meiji Restoration">Meiji Restoration</a> until the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the full title of Japan was <i>Dai Nippon Teikoku</i><span style="font-weight: normal">&#32;(<span lang="ja">\xe5\xa4\xa7\xe6\x97\xa5\xe6\x9c\xac\xe5\xb8\x9d\xe5\x9c\x8b</span>)</span>, meaning "the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Empire of Great Japan</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Today, the name <i>Nihon-koku/Nippon-koku</i><span style="font-weight: normal">&#32;(<span lang="ja">\xe6\x97\xa5\xe6\x9c\xac\xe5\x9b\xbd</span>)</span> is used as a formal modern-day equivalent with the meaning of "the State of Japan". Countries like Japan whose long form does not contain a descriptive designation are generally given a name appended by the character <i>koku</i><span style="font-weight: normal">&#32;(<span lang="ja">\xe5\x9b\xbd</span>)</span>, meaning "country", "nation" or "state".\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Japan" title="History of Japan">History of Japan</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory_and_ancient_history">Prehistory and ancient history</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Emperor_Jimmu.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Emperor_Jimmu.jpg/220px-Emperor_Jimmu.jpg" width="220" height="339" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Emperor_Jimmu.jpg/330px-Emperor_Jimmu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6b/Emperor_Jimmu.jpg 2x" data-file-width="438" data-file-height="674" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Emperor_Jimmu.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Emperor_Jimmu" title="Emperor Jimmu">Emperor Jimmu</a><span style="font-weight: normal">&#32;(<span lang="ja">\xe7\xa5\x9e\xe6\xad\xa6\xe5\xa4\xa9\xe7\x9a\x87</span><span style="display:none">,</span>&#32;<i>Jinmu-tenn\xc5\x8d</i>)</span>, the first <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Japan" title="Emperor of Japan">Emperor of Japan</a> dated as 660 BCE<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> \xe2\x80\x94in modern Japan his accession is marked as <a href="/wiki/National_Foundation_Day" title="National Foundation Day">National Foundation Day</a> on February 11</div></div></div>\n<p>A <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Paleolithic" title="Japanese Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a> culture around 30,000&#160;BC constitutes the first known habitation of the Japanese archipelago. This was followed from around 14,000&#160;BC (the start of the <a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dmon_period" title="J\xc5\x8dmon period">J\xc5\x8dmon period</a>) by a <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> to <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> semi-sedentary <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherer</a> culture characterized by <a href="/wiki/Pit-house" title="Pit-house">pit dwelling</a> and rudimentary agriculture,<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> including by ancestors of contemporary <a href="/wiki/Ainu_people" title="Ainu people">Ainu people</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yamato_people" title="Yamato people">Yamato people</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Decorated clay vessels from this period are some of the oldest surviving examples of pottery in the world. Around 300 BC, the <a href="/wiki/Yayoi_period#Origin_of_the_Yayoi_people" title="Yayoi period">Yayoi people</a> began to enter the Japanese islands, intermingling with the <a href="/wiki/Jomon_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Jomon people">J\xc5\x8dmon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Yayoi_period" title="Yayoi period">Yayoi period</a>, starting around 500&#160;BC, saw the introduction of practices like wet-<a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a> farming,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> a new style of <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy" title="Metallurgy">metallurgy</a>, introduced from China and Korea.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Japan first appears in written history in the Chinese <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Han" title="Book of Han">Book of Han</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Records_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Records of the Three Kingdoms">Records of the Three Kingdoms</a></i>, the most powerful kingdom on the archipelago during the third century was called <a href="/wiki/Yamatai" title="Yamatai">Yamataikoku</a>. Buddhism was introduced to Japan from <a href="/wiki/Baekje" title="Baekje">Baekje</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a> and was promoted by <a href="/wiki/Prince_Sh%C5%8Dtoku" title="Prince Sh\xc5\x8dtoku">Prince Sh\xc5\x8dtoku</a>, but the subsequent development of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Japan" title="Buddhism in Japan">Japanese Buddhism</a> was primarily influenced by China.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Despite early resistance, Buddhism was promoted by the ruling class and gained widespread acceptance beginning in the <a href="/wiki/Asuka_period" title="Asuka period">Asuka period</a> (592\xe2\x80\x93710).<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Nara_period" title="Nara period">Nara period</a> (710\xe2\x80\x93784) marked an emergence of the centralized Japanese state centered on the Imperial Court in <a href="/wiki/Heij%C5%8D_Palace" title="Heij\xc5\x8d Palace">Heij\xc5\x8d-ky\xc5\x8d</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Nara,_Nara" title="Nara, Nara">Nara</a>). The <a href="/wiki/Nara_period" title="Nara period">Nara period</a> is characterized by the appearance of a nascent <a href="/wiki/Japanese_literature" title="Japanese literature">literature</a> as well as the development of Buddhist-inspired art and <a href="/wiki/Historic_Monuments_of_Ancient_Nara" title="Historic Monuments of Ancient Nara">architecture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> epidemic of 735\xe2\x80\x93737 is believed to have killed as much as one-third of Japan\'s population.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> In 784, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Kanmu" title="Emperor Kanmu">Emperor Kanmu</a> moved the capital from <a href="/wiki/Nara,_Nara" title="Nara, Nara">Nara</a> to <a href="/wiki/Nagaoka-ky%C5%8D" title="Nagaoka-ky\xc5\x8d">Nagaoka-ky\xc5\x8d</a>, then to <a href="/wiki/Heian-ky%C5%8D" title="Heian-ky\xc5\x8d">Heian-ky\xc5\x8d</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Kyoto" title="Kyoto">Kyoto</a>) in 794.\n</p><p>This marked the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Heian_period" title="Heian period">Heian period</a> (794\xe2\x80\x931185), during which a distinctly indigenous Japanese culture emerged, noted for its <a href="/wiki/Japanese_art" title="Japanese art">art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_poetry" title="Japanese poetry">poetry</a> and prose. <a href="/wiki/Murasaki_Shikibu" title="Murasaki Shikibu">Murasaki Shikibu</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_Genji" title="The Tale of Genji">The Tale of Genji</a></i> and the lyrics of Japan\'s national anthem "<a href="/wiki/Kimigayo" title="Kimigayo">Kimigayo</a>" were written during this time.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> began to spread during the <a href="/wiki/Heian_era" class="mw-redirect" title="Heian era">Heian era</a> chiefly through two major sects, <a href="/wiki/Tendai" title="Tendai">Tendai</a> by <a href="/wiki/Saich%C5%8D" title="Saich\xc5\x8d">Saich\xc5\x8d</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shingon" class="mw-redirect" title="Shingon">Shingon</a> by <a href="/wiki/K%C5%ABkai" title="K\xc5\xabkai">K\xc5\xabkai</a>. <a href="/wiki/Pure_Land_Buddhism" title="Pure Land Buddhism">Pure Land Buddhism</a> (<a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Ddo-sh%C5%AB" title="J\xc5\x8ddo-sh\xc5\xab">J\xc5\x8ddo-sh\xc5\xab</a>, <a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Ddo_Shinsh%C5%AB" title="J\xc5\x8ddo Shinsh\xc5\xab">J\xc5\x8ddo Shinsh\xc5\xab</a>) became greatly popular in the latter half of the 11th century.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feudal_era">Feudal era</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba_2.jpg/220px-M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba_2.jpg" width="220" height="101" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba_2.jpg/330px-M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba_2.jpg/440px-M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3184" data-file-height="1461" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Samurai" title="Samurai">Samurai</a> warriors facing Mongols during the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_of_Japan" title="Mongol invasions of Japan">Mongol invasions of Japan</a>; <a href="/wiki/Takezaki_Suenaga" title="Takezaki Suenaga">Suenaga</a>, 1293</div></div></div>\n<p>Japan\'s feudal era was characterized by the emergence and dominance of a ruling class of warriors, the <a href="/wiki/Samurai" title="Samurai">samurai</a>. In 1185, following the defeat of the <a href="/wiki/Taira_clan" title="Taira clan">Taira clan</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Genpei_War" title="Genpei War">Genpei War</a>, sung in the epic <a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_the_Heike" title="The Tale of the Heike">Tale of Heike</a>, samurai <a href="/wiki/Minamoto_no_Yoritomo" title="Minamoto no Yoritomo">Minamoto no Yoritomo</a> was appointed <i><a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dgun" title="Sh\xc5\x8dgun">sh\xc5\x8dgun</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Go-Toba" title="Emperor Go-Toba">Emperor Go-Toba</a>, and Yoritomo established a base of power in <a href="/wiki/Kamakura,_Kanagawa" class="mw-redirect" title="Kamakura, Kanagawa">Kamakura</a>. After his death, the <a href="/wiki/H%C5%8Dj%C5%8D_clan" title="H\xc5\x8dj\xc5\x8d clan">H\xc5\x8dj\xc5\x8d clan</a> came to power as regents for the <i>sh\xc5\x8dguns</i>. The <a href="/wiki/Zen" title="Zen">Zen</a> school of Buddhism was introduced from China in the <a href="/wiki/Kamakura_period" title="Kamakura period">Kamakura period</a> (1185\xe2\x80\x931333) and became popular among the samurai class.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Kamakura_shogunate" title="Kamakura shogunate">Kamakura shogunate</a> repelled <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_of_Japan" title="Mongol invasions of Japan">Mongol invasions</a> in 1274 and 1281, but was eventually <a href="/wiki/Kenmu_Restoration" title="Kenmu Restoration">overthrown by Emperor Go-Daigo</a>. <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Go-Daigo" title="Emperor Go-Daigo">Emperor Go-Daigo</a> was himself defeated by <a href="/wiki/Ashikaga_Takauji" title="Ashikaga Takauji">Ashikaga Takauji</a> in 1336.\n</p><p>Ashikaga Takauji established the shogunate in Muromachi, <a href="/wiki/Kyoto" title="Kyoto">Kyoto</a>. This was the start of the <a href="/wiki/Muromachi_period" title="Muromachi period">Muromachi period</a> (1336\xe2\x80\x931573). The <a href="/wiki/Ashikaga_shogunate" title="Ashikaga shogunate">Ashikaga shogunate</a> achieved glory at the age of <a href="/wiki/Ashikaga_Yoshimitsu" title="Ashikaga Yoshimitsu">Ashikaga Yoshimitsu</a>, and the culture based on Zen Buddhism (the art of <i><a href="/wiki/Miyabi" title="Miyabi">Miyabi</a></i>) prospered. This evolved to <a href="/wiki/Higashiyama_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Higashiyama period">Higashiyama Culture</a>, and prospered until the 16th century. On the other hand, the succeeding Ashikaga shogunate failed to control the feudal warlords (<i><a href="/wiki/Daimy%C5%8D" title="Daimy\xc5\x8d">daimy\xc5\x8ds</a></i>) and a civil war (the <a href="/wiki/%C5%8Cnin_War" title="\xc5\x8cnin War">\xc5\x8cnin War</a>) began in 1467, opening the century-long <a href="/wiki/Sengoku_period" title="Sengoku period">Sengoku period</a> ("Warring States").<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the 16th century, traders and <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">Jesuit</a> <a href="/wiki/Missionary" title="Missionary">missionaries</a> from <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> reached Japan for the first time, initiating direct <a href="/wiki/Nanban_trade" title="Nanban trade">commercial</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nanban_art" title="Nanban art">cultural</a> exchange between Japan and the West. This allowed <a href="/wiki/Oda_Nobunaga" title="Oda Nobunaga">Oda Nobunaga</a> to obtain European technology and firearms, which he used to conquer many other <i>daimy\xc5\x8ds</i>. His consolidation of power began what was known as the <a href="/wiki/Azuchi%E2%80%93Momoyama_period" title="Azuchi\xe2\x80\x93Momoyama period">Azuchi\xe2\x80\x93Momoyama period</a> (1573\xe2\x80\x931603). After Nobunaga was assassinated in 1582 by <a href="/wiki/Akechi_Mitsuhide" title="Akechi Mitsuhide">Akechi Mitsuhide</a>, his successor <a href="/wiki/Toyotomi_Hideyoshi" title="Toyotomi Hideyoshi">Toyotomi Hideyoshi</a> unified the nation in 1590 and launched <a href="/wiki/Japanese_invasions_of_Korea_(1592%E2%80%9398)" title="Japanese invasions of Korea (1592\xe2\x80\x9398)">two unsuccessful invasions of Korea in 1592 and 1597</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_Ieyasu" title="Tokugawa Ieyasu">Tokugawa Ieyasu</a> served as regent for Hideyoshi\'s <a href="/wiki/Toyotomi_Hideyori" title="Toyotomi Hideyori">son</a> and used his position to gain political and military support. When open war broke out, Ieyasu defeated rival clans in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sekigahara" title="Battle of Sekigahara">Battle of Sekigahara</a> in 1600. Tokugawa Ieyasu was appointed <i>sh\xc5\x8dgun</i> by <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Go-Y%C5%8Dzei" title="Emperor Go-Y\xc5\x8dzei">Emperor Go-Y\xc5\x8dzei</a> in 1603 and established the <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_shogunate" title="Tokugawa shogunate">Tokugawa shogunate</a> in <a href="/wiki/Edo" title="Edo">Edo</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The shogunate enacted measures including <i><a href="/wiki/Buke_shohatto" title="Buke shohatto">buke shohatto</a></i>, as a code of conduct to control the autonomous <i>daimy\xc5\x8ds</i>;<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> and in 1639 the isolationist <i><a href="/wiki/Sakoku" title="Sakoku">sakoku</a></i> ("closed country") policy that spanned the two and a half centuries of tenuous political unity known as the <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a> (1603\xe2\x80\x931868).<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The study of Western sciences, known as <i><a href="/wiki/Rangaku" title="Rangaku">rangaku</a></i>, continued through contact with the Dutch enclave at <a href="/wiki/Dejima" title="Dejima">Dejima</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nagasaki" title="Nagasaki">Nagasaki</a>. The Edo period also gave rise to <i><a href="/wiki/Kokugaku" title="Kokugaku">kokugaku</a></i> ("national studies"), the study of Japan by the Japanese.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_era">Modern era</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Meiji_emperor_color.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Meiji_emperor_color.jpg/170px-Meiji_emperor_color.jpg" width="170" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Meiji_emperor_color.jpg/255px-Meiji_emperor_color.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Meiji_emperor_color.jpg/340px-Meiji_emperor_color.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="857" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Meiji_emperor_color.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Emperor_Meiji" title="Emperor Meiji">Emperor Meiji</a> (1868\xe2\x80\x931912), in whose name imperial rule was <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Restoration" title="Meiji Restoration">restored</a> at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_shogunate" title="Tokugawa shogunate">Tokugawa shogunate</a></div></div></div>\n<p>On March 31, 1854, <a href="/wiki/Matthew_C._Perry" title="Matthew C. Perry">Commodore Matthew Perry</a> and the "<a href="/wiki/Black_Ships" title="Black Ships">Black Ships</a>" of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> forced the opening of Japan to the outside world with the <a href="/wiki/Convention_of_Kanagawa" title="Convention of Kanagawa">Convention of Kanagawa</a>. Subsequent similar treaties with Western countries in the <a href="/wiki/Bakumatsu" title="Bakumatsu">Bakumatsu</a> period brought economic and political crises. The resignation of the <i>sh\xc5\x8dgun</i> led to the <a href="/wiki/Boshin_War" title="Boshin War">Boshin War</a> and the establishment of a <a href="/wiki/Abolition_of_the_han_system" title="Abolition of the han system">centralized state</a> nominally unified under the Emperor (the <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Restoration" title="Meiji Restoration">Meiji Restoration</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Plunging itself through an active process of Westernization during the Meiji Restoration in 1868, Japan adopted Western political, judicial and military institutions and Western cultural influences integrated with its traditional culture for modern industrialization. The <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_Japan" title="Cabinet of Japan">Cabinet</a> organized the <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council_(Japan)" class="mw-redirect" title="Privy Council (Japan)">Privy Council</a>, introduced the <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Constitution" title="Meiji Constitution">Meiji Constitution</a>, and assembled the <a href="/wiki/National_Diet" title="National Diet">Imperial Diet</a>. The Meiji Restoration transformed the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Empire of Japan</a> into an industrialized world power that pursued military conflict to expand its sphere of influence. Although France and Britain showed some interest, the European powers largely ignored Japan and instead concentrated on the much greater attractions of China. France was also set back by its failures in Mexico and defeat by the Germans.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> After victories in the <a href="/wiki/First_Sino-Japanese_War" title="First Sino-Japanese War">First Sino-Japanese War</a> (1894\xe2\x80\x931895) and the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Japanese_War" title="Russo-Japanese War">Russo-Japanese War</a> (1904\xe2\x80\x931905), Japan gained control of Taiwan, Korea and the southern half of <a href="/wiki/Sakhalin" title="Sakhalin">Sakhalin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> In addition to imperialistic success, Japan also invested much more heavily in its own economic growth, leading to a period of economic flourishing in the country which lasted until the Great Depression.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Japan\'s population grew from 35 million in 1873 to 70 million in 1935.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pacific_Area_-_The_Imperial_Powers_1939_-_Map.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Pacific_Area_-_The_Imperial_Powers_1939_-_Map.svg/220px-Pacific_Area_-_The_Imperial_Powers_1939_-_Map.svg.png" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Pacific_Area_-_The_Imperial_Powers_1939_-_Map.svg/330px-Pacific_Area_-_The_Imperial_Powers_1939_-_Map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Pacific_Area_-_The_Imperial_Powers_1939_-_Map.svg/440px-Pacific_Area_-_The_Imperial_Powers_1939_-_Map.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1039" data-file-height="814" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pacific_Area_-_The_Imperial_Powers_1939_-_Map.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Japanese_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese Empire">Japanese Empire</a> in 1939</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Japan_during_World_War_I" title="Japan during World War I">In World War I</a>, Japan joined the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Allies</a> and captured German possessions, and made advances into China. The early 20th century saw a period of <a href="/wiki/Taish%C5%8D_period" title="Taish\xc5\x8d period">Taish\xc5\x8d democracy</a> (1912\xe2\x80\x931926), but the 1920s saw a fragile democracy buckle under a political shift towards <a href="/wiki/Statism_in_Sh%C5%8Dwa_Japan" title="Statism in Sh\xc5\x8dwa Japan">statism</a>, the passing of <a href="/wiki/Peace_Preservation_Law#Public_Security_Preservation_Law_of_1925" title="Peace Preservation Law">laws against political dissent</a> and a series of <a href="/wiki/May_15_Incident" title="May 15 Incident">attempted coups</a>. This process accelerated during the 1930s, spawning a number of new Radical Nationalist groups which shared a hostility to liberal democracy and a dedication to expansion in Asia. Japanese <a href="/wiki/Expansionism" title="Expansionism">expansionism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_militarism" title="Japanese militarism">militarization</a> along with the totalitarianism and ultranationalism reshaped the country. In 1931 Japan <a href="/wiki/Japanese_invasion_of_Manchuria" title="Japanese invasion of Manchuria">invaded and occupied</a> <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a> and following <a href="/wiki/Lytton_Report" title="Lytton Report">international condemnation of this occupation</a>, it quit the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> in 1933. In 1936, Japan signed the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Comintern_Pact" title="Anti-Comintern Pact">Anti-Comintern Pact</a> with <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Germany</a> and the 1940 <a href="/wiki/Tripartite_Pact" title="Tripartite Pact">Tripartite Pact</a> made it one of the <a href="/wiki/Axis_Powers" class="mw-redirect" title="Axis Powers">Axis Powers</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Umezu.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Umezu.jpg/220px-Umezu.jpg" width="220" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Umezu.jpg/330px-Umezu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Umezu.jpg/440px-Umezu.jpg 2x" data-file-width="687" data-file-height="727" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Umezu.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Japanese officials surrendering to the Allies on September 2, 1945, in Tokyo Bay, ending World War II</div></div></div>\n<p>The Empire of Japan invaded other parts of China in 1937, precipitating the <a href="/wiki/Second_Sino-Japanese_War" title="Second Sino-Japanese War">Second Sino-Japanese War</a> (1937\xe2\x80\x931945). The Imperial Japanese Army <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Nanking" title="Battle of Nanking">swiftly captured</a> the capital <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a> and conducted the <a href="/wiki/Nanking_Massacre" title="Nanking Massacre">Nanking Massacre</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> In 1940, the Empire <a href="/wiki/Japanese_invasion_of_French_Indochina" title="Japanese invasion of French Indochina">invaded French Indochina</a>, after which the United States placed an oil embargo on Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> On December 7\xe2\x80\x938, 1941, Japanese forces carried out surprise <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Attack on Pearl Harbor">attacks on Pearl Harbor</a>, British forces in <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Malaya" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Malaya">Malaya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Singapore_(1941)" title="Bombing of Singapore (1941)">Singapore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hong_Kong" title="Battle of Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_declaration_of_war_on_the_United_States_and_the_British_Empire" title="Japanese declaration of war on the United States and the British Empire">declared war on the United States and the British Empire</a>, bringing the United States and the United Kingdom into <a href="/wiki/Pacific_War" title="Pacific War">World War II in the Pacific</a>. After <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allied</a> victories across the Pacific during the next four years, which culminated in the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_invasion_of_Manchuria" title="Soviet invasion of Manchuria">Soviet invasion of Manchuria</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki</a> in 1945, Japan agreed to an <a href="/wiki/Surrender_of_Japan" title="Surrender of Japan">unconditional surrender</a> on August 15.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The war cost Japan, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_colonial_empire" title="Japanese colonial empire">its colonies</a>, China and the war\'s other combatants tens of millions of lives and left much of Japan\'s industry and infrastructure destroyed. The Allies (led by the United States) repatriated millions of <a href="/wiki/Japanese_diaspora" title="Japanese diaspora">ethnic Japanese</a> from colonies and military camps throughout Asia, largely eliminating the Japanese empire and restoring the independence of its conquered territories.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The Allies also convened the <a href="/wiki/International_Military_Tribunal_for_the_Far_East" title="International Military Tribunal for the Far East">International Military Tribunal for the Far East</a> on May 3, 1946, to prosecute some senior generals for <a href="/wiki/Japanese_war_crimes" title="Japanese war crimes">war crimes</a>.\n</p><p>In 1947, Japan adopted a new <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Japan" title="Constitution of Japan">constitution</a> emphasizing liberal democratic practices. The <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Japan" title="Occupation of Japan">Allied occupation</a> ended with the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_San_Francisco" title="Treaty of San Francisco">Treaty of San Francisco</a> in 1952<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> and Japan was granted membership in the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> in 1956. Japan later achieved <a href="/wiki/Japanese_post-war_economic_miracle" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese post-war economic miracle">rapid growth</a> to become the second-largest economy in the world, until surpassed by China in 2010. This ended in the mid-1990s when Japan suffered a <a href="/wiki/Japanese_asset_price_bubble" title="Japanese asset price bubble">major recession</a>. In the beginning of the 21st century, positive growth has signaled a gradual economic recovery.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> On March 11, 2011, Japan suffered one of the <a href="/wiki/2011_T%C5%8Dhoku_earthquake_and_tsunami" title="2011 T\xc5\x8dhoku earthquake and tsunami">largest earthquakes in its recorded history</a>; this triggered the <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster</a>, one of the worst disasters in the history of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes-tsunami_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes-tsunami-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Japan" title="Geography of Japan">Geography of Japan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Japan" title="Geology of Japan">Geology of Japan</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Satellite_View_of_Japan_1999.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Satellite_View_of_Japan_1999.jpg/220px-Satellite_View_of_Japan_1999.jpg" width="220" height="281" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Satellite_View_of_Japan_1999.jpg/330px-Satellite_View_of_Japan_1999.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Satellite_View_of_Japan_1999.jpg/440px-Satellite_View_of_Japan_1999.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1638" data-file-height="2092" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Satellite_View_of_Japan_1999.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Japanese_archipelago" title="Japanese archipelago">Japanese archipelago</a> as seen from satellite</div></div></div>\n<p>Japan has a total of 6,852 islands extending along the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_coast" title="Pacific coast">Pacific coast</a>. It is over 3,000&#160;km (1,900&#160;mi) long from the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Okhotsk" title="Sea of Okhotsk">Sea of Okhotsk</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Sea" title="Philippine Sea">Philippine Sea</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The country, including all of the islands it controls, lies between latitudes 24\xc2\xb0 and 46\xc2\xb0N, and longitudes 122\xc2\xb0 and 146\xc2\xb0E. The main islands, from north to south, are <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a>, <a href="/wiki/Honshu" title="Honshu">Honshu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shikoku" title="Shikoku">Shikoku</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kyushu" title="Kyushu">Kyushu</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands" title="Ryukyu Islands">Ryukyu Islands</a>, which include <a href="/wiki/Okinawa_Island" title="Okinawa Island">Okinawa</a>, are a chain to the south of <a href="/wiki/Kyushu" title="Kyushu">Kyushu</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Nanp%C5%8D_Islands" title="Nanp\xc5\x8d Islands">Nanp\xc5\x8d Islands</a> are south of the main islands of Japan. Together they are often known as the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_archipelago" title="Japanese archipelago">Japanese archipelago</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> As of 2018, Japan\'s territory is 377,973.89&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (145,936.53&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Geospatial_Information_Authority_of_Japan_2018_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geospatial_Information_Authority_of_Japan_2018-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> It is the largest <a href="/wiki/Island_country" title="Island country">island country</a> in <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>. Japan has the sixth longest <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_length_of_coastline" title="List of countries by length of coastline">coastline</a> in the world (29,751&#160;km (18,486&#160;mi)). It doesn\'t have land borders. Due to its many far-flung outlying islands, Japan has the eighth largest <a href="/wiki/Exclusive_economic_zone_of_Japan" title="Exclusive economic zone of Japan">Exclusive Economic Zone</a> in the world covering 4,470,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (1,730,000&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>About 73 percent of Japan is forested, mountainous and unsuitable for <a href="/wiki/Agriculture,_forestry,_and_fishing_in_Japan" title="Agriculture, forestry, and fishing in Japan">agricultural</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing_in_Japan" title="Manufacturing in Japan">industrial</a> or <a href="/wiki/Housing_in_Japan" title="Housing in Japan">residential</a> use.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> As a result, the habitable zones, mainly located in coastal areas, have extremely high population densities. Japan is one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_population_density" class="mw-redirect" title="List of countries by population density">most densely populated countries</a> in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The islands of Japan are located in a <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanic</a> zone on the <a href="/wiki/Ring_of_Fire" title="Ring of Fire">Pacific Ring of Fire</a>. They are primarily the result of large oceanic movements occurring over hundreds of millions of years from the mid-Silurian to the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Subduction" title="Subduction">subduction</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Sea_Plate" title="Philippine Sea Plate">Philippine Sea Plate</a> beneath the continental <a href="/wiki/Amurian_Plate" title="Amurian Plate">Amurian Plate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Okinawa_Plate" title="Okinawa Plate">Okinawa Plate</a> to the south, and subduction of the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Plate" title="Pacific Plate">Pacific Plate</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Okhotsk_Plate" title="Okhotsk Plate">Okhotsk Plate</a> to the north. The <a href="/wiki/Boso_Triple_Junction" title="Boso Triple Junction">Boso Triple Junction</a> off the coast of Japan is a triple junction where the <a href="/wiki/North_American_Plate" title="North American Plate">North American Plate</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Plate" title="Pacific Plate">Pacific Plate</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Sea_Plate" title="Philippine Sea Plate">Philippine Sea Plate</a> meets. Japan was originally attached to the eastern coast of the Eurasian continent. The subducting plates pulled Japan eastward, opening the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Japan" title="Sea of Japan">Sea of Japan</a> around 15 million years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Japan has 108 active volcanoes. During the twentieth century several new volcanoes emerged, including <a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dwa-shinzan" title="Sh\xc5\x8dwa-shinzan">Sh\xc5\x8dwa-shinzan</a> on Hokkaido and <a href="/wiki/My%C5%8Djin-sh%C5%8D" title="My\xc5\x8djin-sh\xc5\x8d">My\xc5\x8djin-sh\xc5\x8d</a> off the <a href="/wiki/Bayonnaise_Rocks" title="Bayonnaise Rocks">Bayonnaise Rocks</a> in the Pacific. Destructive earthquakes, often resulting in <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunami</a>, occur several times each century.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/1923_Great_Kant%C5%8D_earthquake" title="1923 Great Kant\xc5\x8d earthquake">1923 Tokyo earthquake</a> killed over 140,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> More recent major quakes are the 1995 <a href="/wiki/Great_Hanshin_earthquake" title="Great Hanshin earthquake">Great Hanshin earthquake</a> and the <a href="/wiki/2011_T%C5%8Dhoku_earthquake_and_tsunami" title="2011 T\xc5\x8dhoku earthquake and tsunami">2011 T\xc5\x8dhoku earthquake</a>, a 9.1-magnitude<sup id="cite_ref-USGS9.1_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGS9.1-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> quake which hit Japan on March 11, 2011, and triggered a large tsunami.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes-tsunami_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes-tsunami-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Japan is substantially prone to earthquakes, tsunami and volcanoes due to its location along the Pacific Ring of Fire.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> It has the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_natural_disaster_risk" title="List of countries by natural disaster risk">15th highest natural disaster risk</a> as measured in the 2013 World Risk Index.<sup id="cite_ref-2013_World_Risk_Report_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2013_World_Risk_Report-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Japan#Climate" title="Geography of Japan">Geography of Japan \xc2\xa7&#160;Climate</a></div>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Cherry_blossoms_at_Yoshinoyama_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Cherry_blossoms_at_Yoshinoyama_01.jpg/200px-Cherry_blossoms_at_Yoshinoyama_01.jpg" width="200" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Cherry_blossoms_at_Yoshinoyama_01.jpg/300px-Cherry_blossoms_at_Yoshinoyama_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Cherry_blossoms_at_Yoshinoyama_01.jpg/400px-Cherry_blossoms_at_Yoshinoyama_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1500" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Cherry_blossom" title="Cherry blossom">Cherry blossoms</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Yoshino" title="Mount Yoshino">Mount Yoshino</a> have been the subject of many plays and <a href="/wiki/Waka_(poetry)" title="Waka (poetry)">waka poetry</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Kongobuji_Koyasan07n3200.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Kongobuji_Koyasan07n3200.jpg/200px-Kongobuji_Koyasan07n3200.jpg" width="200" height="133" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Kongobuji_Koyasan07n3200.jpg/300px-Kongobuji_Koyasan07n3200.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Kongobuji_Koyasan07n3200.jpg/400px-Kongobuji_Koyasan07n3200.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3200" data-file-height="2133" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Autumn <a href="/wiki/Maple_tree" class="mw-redirect" title="Maple tree">maple leaves</a> (<a href="/wiki/Momiji" class="mw-redirect" title="Momiji">momiji</a>) at <a href="/wiki/Kong%C5%8Dbu-ji" title="Kong\xc5\x8dbu-ji">Kong\xc5\x8dbu-ji</a> on <a href="/wiki/Mount_K%C5%8Dya" title="Mount K\xc5\x8dya">Mount K\xc5\x8dya</a>, a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" class="mw-redirect" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a></div></div></div></div>\n<p>The climate of Japan is predominantly temperate, but varies greatly from north to south. Japan\'s geographical features divide it into six principal climatic zones: <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Japan" title="Sea of Japan">Sea of Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Highland_(Japan)" title="Central Highland (Japan)">Central Highland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seto_Inland_Sea" title="Seto Inland Sea">Seto Inland Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands" title="Ryukyu Islands">Ryukyu Islands</a>. The northernmost zone, Hokkaido, has a <a href="/wiki/Humid_continental_climate" title="Humid continental climate">humid continental climate</a> with long, cold winters and very warm to cool summers. <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">Precipitation</a> is not heavy, but the islands usually develop deep snowbanks in the winter.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the Sea of Japan zone on Honshu\'s west coast, northwest winter winds bring heavy snowfall. In the summer, the region is cooler than the Pacific area, though it sometimes experiences extremely hot temperatures because of the <a href="/wiki/Foehn_wind" title="Foehn wind">foehn</a>. The Central Highland has a typical inland humid continental climate, with large temperature differences between summer and winter seasons, as well as large diurnal variation; precipitation is light, though winters are usually snowy. The mountains of the <a href="/wiki/Ch%C5%ABgoku_region" title="Ch\xc5\xabgoku region">Ch\xc5\xabgoku</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shikoku" title="Shikoku">Shikoku</a> regions shelter the <a href="/wiki/Seto_Inland_Sea" title="Seto Inland Sea">Seto Inland Sea</a> from seasonal winds, bringing mild weather year-round.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Pacific coast features a <a href="/wiki/Humid_subtropical" class="mw-redirect" title="Humid subtropical">humid subtropical</a> climate that experiences milder winters with occasional snowfall and hot, humid summers because of the southeast seasonal wind. The <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands" title="Ryukyu Islands">Ryukyu Islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nanp%C5%8D_Islands" title="Nanp\xc5\x8d Islands">Nanp\xc5\x8d Islands</a> have a <a href="/wiki/Subtropics" title="Subtropics">subtropical climate</a>, with warm winters and hot summers. Precipitation is very heavy, especially during the rainy season.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_81-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The average winter temperature in Japan is 5.1&#160;\xc2\xb0C (41.2&#160;\xc2\xb0F) and the average summer temperature is 25.2&#160;\xc2\xb0C (77.4&#160;\xc2\xb0F).<sup id="cite_ref-climate_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-climate-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The highest temperature ever measured in Japan 41.1&#160;\xc2\xb0C (106.0&#160;\xc2\xb0F) was recorded on July 23, 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The main <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_rainy_season" title="East Asian rainy season">rainy season</a> begins in early May in Okinawa, and the rain front gradually moves north until reaching Hokkaido in late July. In most of Honshu, the rainy season begins before the middle of June and lasts about six weeks. In late summer and early autumn, <a href="/wiki/Typhoon" title="Typhoon">typhoons</a> often bring heavy rain.<sup id="cite_ref-climate_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-climate-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity">Biodiversity</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_of_Japan" title="Wildlife of Japan">Wildlife of Japan</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jigokudani_hotspring_in_Nagano_Japan_001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Jigokudani_hotspring_in_Nagano_Japan_001.jpg/220px-Jigokudani_hotspring_in_Nagano_Japan_001.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Jigokudani_hotspring_in_Nagano_Japan_001.jpg/330px-Jigokudani_hotspring_in_Nagano_Japan_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Jigokudani_hotspring_in_Nagano_Japan_001.jpg/440px-Jigokudani_hotspring_in_Nagano_Japan_001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jigokudani_hotspring_in_Nagano_Japan_001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Japanese_macaque" title="Japanese macaque">Japanese macaques</a> at <a href="/wiki/Jigokudani_Monkey_Park" title="Jigokudani Monkey Park">Jigokudani hot spring</a> are notable for visiting the spa in the winter</div></div></div>\n<p>Japan has nine forest <a href="/wiki/Ecoregions_in_Japan" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecoregions in Japan">ecoregions</a> which reflect the climate and geography of the islands. They range from <a href="/wiki/Tropical_and_subtropical_moist_broadleaf_forests" title="Tropical and subtropical moist broadleaf forests">subtropical moist broadleaf forests</a> in the Ry\xc5\xabky\xc5\xab and <a href="/wiki/Bonin_Islands" title="Bonin Islands">Bonin Islands</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Temperate_broadleaf_and_mixed_forests" class="mw-redirect" title="Temperate broadleaf and mixed forests">temperate broadleaf and mixed forests</a> in the mild climate regions of the main islands, to <a href="/wiki/Temperate_coniferous_forest" title="Temperate coniferous forest">temperate coniferous forests</a> in the cold, winter portions of the northern islands.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Japan has over 90,000 species of <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_of_Japan" title="Wildlife of Japan">wildlife</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Brown_bear" title="Brown bear">brown bear</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_macaque" title="Japanese macaque">Japanese macaque</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_raccoon_dog" title="Japanese raccoon dog">Japanese raccoon dog</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Large_Japanese_field_mouse" title="Large Japanese field mouse">large Japanese field mouse</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_giant_salamander" title="Japanese giant salamander">Japanese giant salamander</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> A large network of <a href="/wiki/List_of_national_parks_of_Japan" title="List of national parks of Japan">national parks</a> has been established to protect important areas of flora and fauna as well as thirty-seven <a href="/wiki/Ramsar_sites_in_Japan" class="mw-redirect" title="Ramsar sites in Japan">Ramsar wetland sites</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Sites_in_Japan" class="mw-redirect" title="World Heritage Sites in Japan">Four sites</a> have been inscribed on the UNESCO World Heritage List for their outstanding natural value.<sup id="cite_ref-unesco1_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unesco1-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environment">Environment</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_issues_in_Japan" title="Environmental issues in Japan">Environmental issues in Japan</a></div>\n<p>In the period of rapid economic growth after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, environmental policies were downplayed by the government and industrial corporations; as a result, <a href="/wiki/Four_Big_Pollution_Diseases_of_Japan" title="Four Big Pollution Diseases of Japan">environmental pollution</a> was widespread in the 1950s and 1960s. Responding to rising concern about the problem, the government introduced several environmental protection laws in 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">oil crisis in 1973</a> also encouraged the efficient use of energy because of Japan\'s lack of natural resources.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Current environmental issues include urban air pollution (<a href="/wiki/NOx" title="NOx">NOx</a>, suspended particulate matter, and toxics), <a href="/wiki/Waste_management" title="Waste management">waste management</a>, water <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">eutrophication</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nature_conservation" class="mw-redirect" title="Nature conservation">nature conservation</a>, climate change, chemical management and international co-operation for conservation.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As of June 2015, more than 40 coal-fired power plants are planned or under construction in Japan. The NGO Climate Action Network announced Japan as the winner of its "Fossil of the Day" award for "doing the most to block progress on climate action".<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Japan ranks 20th in the 2018 <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Performance_Index" title="Environmental Performance Index">Environmental Performance Index</a>, which measures a nation\'s commitment to environmental sustainability.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> As the host and signatory of the 1997 <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol</a>, Japan is under treaty obligation to reduce its carbon dioxide emissions and to take other steps to curb climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Japan" title="Politics of Japan">Politics of Japan</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Japan" title="Government of Japan">Government of Japan</a></div>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:left; float:right; margin-right:9px; margin-left:2px;">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<td style="text-align:left;"><a href="/wiki/File:Emperor_Akihito_cropped_2_Barack_Obama_Emperor_Akihito_and_Empress_Michiko_20140424_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Emperor Akihito cropped 2 Barack Obama Emperor Akihito and Empress Michiko 20140424 1.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Emperor_Akihito_cropped_2_Barack_Obama_Emperor_Akihito_and_Empress_Michiko_20140424_1.jpg/126px-Emperor_Akihito_cropped_2_Barack_Obama_Emperor_Akihito_and_Empress_Michiko_20140424_1.jpg" width="126" height="177" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Emperor_Akihito_cropped_2_Barack_Obama_Emperor_Akihito_and_Empress_Michiko_20140424_1.jpg/189px-Emperor_Akihito_cropped_2_Barack_Obama_Emperor_Akihito_and_Empress_Michiko_20140424_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Emperor_Akihito_cropped_2_Barack_Obama_Emperor_Akihito_and_Empress_Michiko_20140424_1.jpg/252px-Emperor_Akihito_cropped_2_Barack_Obama_Emperor_Akihito_and_Empress_Michiko_20140424_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="662" data-file-height="930" /></a></td>\n<td style="text-align:left;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shinzo_Abe_(2017).jpg" class="image"><img alt="Shinzo Abe (2017).jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Shinzo_Abe_%282017%29.jpg/140px-Shinzo_Abe_%282017%29.jpg" width="140" height="177" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Shinzo_Abe_%282017%29.jpg/210px-Shinzo_Abe_%282017%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Shinzo_Abe_%282017%29.jpg/280px-Shinzo_Abe_%282017%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="527" data-file-height="666" /></a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/Akihito" title="Akihito">Akihito</a> <br /><small><a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Japan" title="Emperor of Japan">Emperor</a> since 1989</small>\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/Shinz%C5%8D_Abe" title="Shinz\xc5\x8d Abe">Shinz\xc5\x8d Abe</a><br /><small><a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Japan" title="Prime Minister of Japan">Prime Minister</a> since 2012</small>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Japan is a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a> whereby the power of the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Japan" title="Emperor of Japan">Emperor</a> is very limited. As a ceremonial figurehead, he is defined by the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Japan" title="Constitution of Japan">constitution</a> to be "the symbol of the State and of the unity of the people". Executive power is wielded chiefly by the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Japan" title="Prime Minister of Japan">Prime Minister</a> and his <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_Japan" title="Cabinet of Japan">cabinet</a>, while sovereignty is vested in the Japanese people.<sup id="cite_ref-Constitution_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Japan\'s legislative body is the <a href="/wiki/National_Diet" title="National Diet">National Diet</a>, seated in <a href="/wiki/Chiyoda,_Tokyo" title="Chiyoda, Tokyo">Chiyoda, Tokyo</a>. The Diet is a <a href="/wiki/Bicameralism" title="Bicameralism">bicameral</a> body, comprising the lower <a href="/wiki/House_of_Representatives_(Japan)" title="House of Representatives (Japan)">House of Representatives</a> with 465 seats, elected by popular vote every four years or when dissolved; and the upper <a href="/wiki/House_of_Councillors_(Japan)" title="House of Councillors (Japan)">House of Councillors</a> with 242 seats, whose popularly elected members serve six-year terms. There is <a href="/wiki/Universal_suffrage" title="Universal suffrage">universal suffrage</a> for adults over 18 years of age,<sup id="cite_ref-tst-_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tst--96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> with a <a href="/wiki/Secret_ballot" title="Secret ballot">secret ballot</a> for all elected offices.<sup id="cite_ref-Constitution_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> The Diet is dominated by the social liberal <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Democratic_Party_of_Japan" title="Constitutional Democratic Party of Japan">Constitutional Democratic Party</a> (CDP) and the conservative <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democratic_Party_(Japan)" title="Liberal Democratic Party (Japan)">Liberal Democratic Party</a> (LDP). The LDP has enjoyed near-continuous electoral success since 1955, except for brief periods between 1993 and 1994 and from 2009 to 2012. As of November 2017, it holds 283 seats in the lower house and 125 seats in the upper house.\n</p><p>The Prime Minister of Japan is the <a href="/wiki/Head_of_government" title="Head of government">head of government</a> and is <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Investiture" title="Imperial Investiture">appointed</a> by the Emperor after being designated by the Diet from among its members. The Prime Minister is the head of the Cabinet, and appoints and dismisses the <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_State" title="Minister of State">Ministers of State</a>. Following the LDP\'s landslide victory in the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_general_election,_2012" title="Japanese general election, 2012">2012 general election</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shinz%C5%8D_Abe" title="Shinz\xc5\x8d Abe">Shinz\xc5\x8d Abe</a> replaced <a href="/wiki/Yoshihiko_Noda" title="Yoshihiko Noda">Yoshihiko Noda</a> as the Prime Minister on December 26, 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Historically influenced by <a href="/wiki/Chinese_law" title="Chinese law">Chinese law</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Japan" title="Law of Japan">Japanese legal system</a> developed independently during the <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a> through texts such as <i><a href="/wiki/Kujikata_Osadamegaki" title="Kujikata Osadamegaki">Kujikata Osadamegaki</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> However, since the late 19th century the <a href="/wiki/Judicial_system_of_Japan" title="Judicial system of Japan">judicial system</a> has been largely based on the <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law</a> of Europe, notably Germany. For example, in 1896, the Japanese government established a civil code based on a draft of the German <a href="/wiki/B%C3%BCrgerliches_Gesetzbuch" title="B\xc3\xbcrgerliches Gesetzbuch">B\xc3\xbcrgerliches Gesetzbuch</a>; with the code remaining in effect with post\xe2\x80\x93World War II modifications.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Statutory law originates in Japan\'s legislature and has the <a href="/wiki/Rubber_stamp_(politics)" title="Rubber stamp (politics)">rubber stamp</a> of the Emperor. Japan\'s court system is divided into four basic tiers: the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Japan" title="Supreme Court of Japan">Supreme Court</a> and three levels of lower courts.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> The main body of Japanese statutory law is called the <a href="/wiki/Six_Codes" title="Six Codes">Six Codes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_Japan" title="Administrative divisions of Japan">Administrative divisions of Japan</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Prefectures_of_Japan" title="Prefectures of Japan">Prefectures of Japan</a></div>\n<p>Japan is divided into 47 prefectures, each overseen by an elected <a href="/wiki/Governor_(Japan)" title="Governor (Japan)">governor</a>, legislature and administrative bureaucracy.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Each prefecture is further divided into cities, towns and villages.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The nation is currently undergoing administrative <a href="/wiki/Merger_and_dissolution_of_municipalities_of_Japan" class="mw-redirect" title="Merger and dissolution of municipalities of Japan">reorganization by merging</a> many of the cities, towns and villages with each other. This process will reduce the number of sub-prefecture administrative regions and is expected to cut administrative costs.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><div class="noresize" style="height: 662px; width: 500px; "><map name="ImageMap_1_1881368230"><area href="/wiki/Ibaraki_Prefecture" shape="poly" coords="367,377,367,395,351,410,369,421,385,415,385,377" alt="Ibaraki Prefecture" title="Ibaraki Prefecture" /><area href="/wiki/Nagano_Prefecture" shape="poly" coords="316,390,307,381,282,395,282,460,293,461,307,430,319,425,306,401" alt="Nagano Prefecture" title="Nagano Prefecture" /><area href="/wiki/Saitama_Prefecture" shape="poly" coords="320,425,334,410,352,411,359,425,334,426,331,431" alt="Saitama Prefecture" title="Saitama Prefecture" /><area href="/wiki/Chiba_Prefecture" shape="poly" href="/wiki/Miyazaki_Prefecture" shape="poly" coords="70,579,93,578,76,642,53,613,65,607,61,591" alt="Miyazaki Prefecture" title="Miyazaki Prefecture" /><area href="/wiki/Kagoshima_Prefecture" title="T&#x14d;ky&#x14d; Metropolis" /><area href="/wiki/Kanagawa_Prefecture" shape="rect" coords="337,484,384,495" alt="Kanagawa Prefecture" title="Kanagawa Prefecture" /><area href="/wiki/Osaka_Prefecture" shape="rect" coords="193,538,222,550" alt="&#x14c;saka Prefecture" title="&#x14c;saka Prefecture" /><area href="/wiki/Wakayama_Prefecture" shape="rect" usemap="#ImageMap_1_1881368230" /><div style="margin-left: 0px; margin-top: -20px; text-align: left;"><a href="/wiki/File:Regions_and_Prefectures_of_Japan_2.svg" title="About this image"><img alt="About this image" src="/w/extensions/ImageMap/desc-20.png?15600" style="border: none;" /></a></div></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Japan" title="Foreign relations of Japan">Foreign relations of Japan</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Demis-kurils-russian_names.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Demis-kurils-russian_names.png/220px-Demis-kurils-russian_names.png" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Demis-kurils-russian_names.png/330px-Demis-kurils-russian_names.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Demis-kurils-russian_names.png/440px-Demis-kurils-russian_names.png 2x" data-file-width="663" data-file-height="462" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Demis-kurils-russian_names.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Kuril_Islands" title="Kuril Islands">Kuril Islands</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Kuril_Islands_dispute" title="Kuril Islands dispute">disputed islands</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Japan has diplomatic relations with nearly all independent nations and has been an active member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> since December 1956. Japan is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Group_of_Eight_(G8)" class="mw-redirect" title="Group of Eight (G8)">G8</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asia-Pacific_Economic_Cooperation" title="Asia-Pacific Economic Cooperation">APEC</a>, and "<a href="/wiki/ASEAN_Free_Trade_Area#ASEAN_Plus_Three" title="ASEAN Free Trade Area">ASEAN Plus Three</a>", and is a participant in the <a href="/wiki/East_Asia_Summit" title="East Asia Summit">East Asia Summit</a>. Japan signed a security pact with <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> in March 2007<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> and with <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> in October 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> It is the world\'s fifth largest donor of <a href="/wiki/Official_development_assistance" title="Official development assistance">official development assistance</a>, donating US$9.2 billion in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> In 2017, Japan had the fifth largest diplomatic network in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-global-diplomacy-index_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-global-diplomacy-index-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Japan has close ties to the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. Since Japan\'s defeat by the United States and allies in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the two countries have maintained close economic and defense relations. The United States is a major market for Japanese exports and the primary source of Japanese imports, and is committed to defending the country, having military bases in Japan for partially that purpose.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> After Japan\'s defeat in World War II, the Japanese-ruled <a href="/wiki/Northern_Mariana_Islands" title="Northern Mariana Islands">Northern Mariana Islands</a> came under control of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>Japan contests <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>\'s control of the <a href="/wiki/Kuril_Islands_dispute" title="Kuril Islands dispute">Southern Kuril Islands</a> (including Etorofu, Kunashiri, Shikotan, and the Habomai group) which were occupied by the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> in 1945.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>\'s control of <a href="/wiki/Liancourt_Rocks" title="Liancourt Rocks">Liancourt Rocks</a> (Japanese: <i>Takeshima</i>, Korean: <i>Dokdo</i>) are acknowledged, but not accepted and are claimed by Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-takeshima_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-takeshima-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Japan has strained relations with the <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">People\'s Republic of China</a> (PRC) and the <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Republic of China</a> (ROC) over the <a href="/wiki/Senkaku_Islands" title="Senkaku Islands">Senkaku Islands</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> and with the People\'s Republic of China over the status of <a href="/wiki/Okinotorishima" title="Okinotorishima">Okinotorishima</a>.\n</p><p>Japan\'s relationship with South Korea has been strained due to Japan\'s treatment of Koreans during <a href="/wiki/Korea_under_Japanese_rule" title="Korea under Japanese rule">Japanese colonial rule</a>, particularly over the issue of <a href="/wiki/Comfort_women" title="Comfort women">comfort women</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> These women were essentially sex slaves, and although there is no exact number on how many women were subjected to this treatment, experts believe it could be in the tens or hundreds of thousands. Between 1910 and 1945, the Japanese government rebuilt Korean infrastructure. Despite this, modernization in Korea was always linked to Japanese interests and therefore did not imply a "revolutionization" of social structures. For instance, Japan kept Korea\'s primitive feudalistic agriculture because it served Japanese interests.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Further developments on Japan\'s imperialism in Korea included establishing a slew of police stations all over the country, replacing taxes in kind with taxes in fixed money, and taking much of the communal land which had belonged to villages to give them to private companies in Japan<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> (causing many peasants to lose their land.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>) Japan also introduced over 800,000 Japanese immigrants onto the peninsula and carried out a campaign of cultural suppression through efforts to ban the Korean language in schools and force Koreans to adopt Japanese names.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> With the surrender of Japan and the Axis at the end of WWII in 1945, the Korean Peninsula was once again independent. Despite their historical tensions, in December 2015, Japan agreed to settle the comfort women dispute with South Korea by issuing a formal apology, taking responsibility for the issue and paying money to the surviving comfort women. Today, South Korea and Japan have a stronger and more economically-driven relationship. Since the 1990s, the <a href="/wiki/Korean_Wave" title="Korean Wave">Korean Wave</a> has created a large fanbase in East Asia, but most notably in Japan. Japan is the number one importer of Korean music (<a href="/wiki/K-pop" title="K-pop">K-pop</a>), television (<a href="/wiki/Korean_drama" title="Korean drama">K-dramas</a>), and films, but this was only made possible after the South Korean government lifted the 30-year ban on cultural exchange with Japan that had been in place since 1948.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Korean pop cultural products\' success in the Japanese market is partially explained by the borrowing of Japanese ideas such as the star-marketing system and heavy promotion of new television shows and music. Korean dramas such as <i><a href="/wiki/Winter_Sonata" title="Winter Sonata">Winter Sonata</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_1st_Shop_of_Coffee_Prince" title="The 1st Shop of Coffee Prince">Coffee Prince</a>,</i> as well as K-pop artists such as <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_(South_Korean_band)" title="Big Bang (South Korean band)">BIGBANG</a> and <a href="/wiki/SHINee" class="mw-redirect" title="SHINee">SHINee</a> are extremely popular with Japanese consumers. Most recently, South Korean President Moon Jae-in met with Japanese Prime Minister Shinzo Abe at the <a href="/wiki/2017_G20_Hamburg_summit" title="2017 G20 Hamburg summit">2017 G-20 Summit</a> in Hamburg, Germany to discuss the future of their relationship and specifically how to cooperate on finding solutions for <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korean</a> aggression in the region. Both leaders restated their commitment to solving the comfort women dispute, building positive relations in the region, and pressuring China to be more assertive with North Korea as it continues to test <a href="/wiki/North_Korea_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction" title="North Korea and weapons of mass destruction">nuclear weapons</a> and isolate themselves further form the international community.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Japan_Self-Defense_Forces" title="Japan Self-Defense Forces">Japan Self-Defense Forces</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div
