Former Japanese Prime Minister and current Finance Minister <a href="/wiki/Tar%C5%8D_As%C5%8D" title="Tar\xc5\x8d As\xc5\x8d">Tar\xc5\x8d As\xc5\x8d</a> described Japan as being a nation of "one race, one civilization, one language and one culture", which drew criticism from representatives of ethnic minorities such as the <a href="/wiki/Ainu_people" title="Ainu people">Ainu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Japan has the second longest overall <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a> at birth of any country in the world: 83.5 years for persons born in the period 2010\xe2\x80\x932015.<sup id="cite_ref-Table_A.17_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Table_A.17-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-haaretz.com_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haaretz.com-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Aging_of_Japan" title="Aging of Japan">Japanese population is rapidly aging</a> as a result of a <a href="/wiki/Post%E2%80%93World_War_II_baby_boom" class="mw-redirect" title="Post\xe2\x80\x93World War II baby boom">post\xe2\x80\x93World War II baby boom</a> followed by a decrease in birth rates. In 2012, about 24.1 percent of the population was over 65, and the proportion is projected to rise to almost 40 percent by 2050.<sup id="cite_ref-handbook_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-handbook-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 15 September 2018, for the first time, 1 in 5 persons in Japan is 70 or older according to the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Internal_Affairs_and_Communications" title="Ministry of Internal Affairs and Communications">Ministry of Internal Affairs and Communications</a>. 26.18 million people are 70 or older and accounted for 20.7 percent of the population. Elderly women crossed the 20 million line at 20.12 million, substantially outnumbering the nation\xe2\x80\x99s 15.45 million elderly men.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><br />\n</p>\n<table class="navbox">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="10" class="navbox-title"><div style="float:right; width:6em; style="text-align:right;">716,328\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Japan" title="Religion in Japan">Religion in Japan</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Itsukushima_Gate.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Itsukushima_Gate.jpg/220px-Itsukushima_Gate.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Itsukushima_Gate.jpg/330px-Itsukushima_Gate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Itsukushima_Gate.jpg/440px-Itsukushima_Gate.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4200" data-file-height="2803" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Itsukushima_Gate.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Torii" title="Torii">torii</a> of <a href="/wiki/Itsukushima_Shrine" title="Itsukushima Shrine">Itsukushima Shinto Shrine</a> near <a href="/wiki/Hiroshima" title="Hiroshima">Hiroshima</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/Three_Views_of_Japan" title="Three Views of Japan">Three Views of Japan</a> and a UNESCO World Heritage Site</div></div></div>\n<p>Japan has full religious freedom based on Article 20 of its <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Japan" title="Constitution of Japan">Constitution</a>. Upper estimates suggest that 84\xe2\x80\x9396 percent of the Japanese population subscribe to <a href="/wiki/Shinto" title="Shinto">Shinto</a> as its indigenous religion (50% to 80% of which considering degrees of syncretism with <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Japan" title="Buddhism in Japan">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shinbutsu-sh%C5%ABg%C5%8D" title="Shinbutsu-sh\xc5\xabg\xc5\x8d">shinbutsu-sh\xc5\xabg\xc5\x8d</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReischauerJansen215_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReischauerJansen215-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> However, these estimates are based on people <a href="/wiki/Danka_system" title="Danka system">affiliated</a> with a temple, rather than the number of true believers. The number of <a href="/wiki/Shinto_shrine" title="Shinto shrine">Shinto shrines</a> in Japan is estimated to be around 100,000. Other studies have suggested that only 30 percent of the population identify themselves as belonging to a religion.<sup id="cite_ref-Kisala_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kisala-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Edwin_O._Reischauer" title="Edwin O. Reischauer">Edwin Reischauer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marius_Jansen" title="Marius Jansen">Marius Jansen</a>, some 70\xe2\x80\x9380% of the Japanese do not consider themselves believers in any religion. Nevertheless, the level of participation remains high, especially during <a href="/wiki/Japanese_festivals" title="Japanese festivals">festivals</a> and occasions such as the <a href="/wiki/Hatsum%C5%8Dde" title="Hatsum\xc5\x8dde">first shrine visit</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_New_Year" title="Japanese New Year">New Year</a>. <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a> from China have also influenced Japanese beliefs and customs.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> Japanese streets are decorated on <a href="/wiki/Tanabata" title="Tanabata">Tanabata</a>, <a href="/wiki/Obon" class="mw-redirect" title="Obon">Obon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Christmas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReischauerJansen215_252-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReischauerJansen215-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Phoenix_Hall,_Byodo-in,_November_2016_-01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Phoenix_Hall%2C_Byodo-in%2C_November_2016_-01.jpg/220px-Phoenix_Hall%2C_Byodo-in%2C_November_2016_-01.jpg" width="220" height="115" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Phoenix_Hall%2C_Byodo-in%2C_November_2016_-01.jpg/330px-Phoenix_Hall%2C_Byodo-in%2C_November_2016_-01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Phoenix_Hall%2C_Byodo-in%2C_November_2016_-01.jpg/440px-Phoenix_Hall%2C_Byodo-in%2C_November_2016_-01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5377" data-file-height="2812" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Phoenix_Hall,_Byodo-in,_November_2016_-01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/By%C5%8Dd%C5%8D-in" title="By\xc5\x8dd\xc5\x8d-in">By\xc5\x8dd\xc5\x8d-in</a> Buddhist temple, located in <a href="/wiki/Uji,_Kyoto" class="mw-redirect" title="Uji, Kyoto">Uji, Kyoto</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Shinto is the largest religion in Japan, practiced by nearly 80% of the population, yet only a small percentage of these identify themselves as "Shintoists" in surveys. This is due to the fact that "Shinto" has different meanings in Japan: most of the Japanese attend Shinto shrines and beseech <a href="/wiki/Kami" title="Kami">kami</a> without belonging to Shinto organisations, and since there are no formal rituals to become a member of folk Shinto, Shinto membership is often estimated counting those who join organised Shinto sects. Shinto has 100,000 <a href="/wiki/Shinto_shrine" title="Shinto shrine">shrines</a> and 78,890 <a href="/wiki/Kannushi" title="Kannushi">priests</a> in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-BY65_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BY65-255">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> first arrived in Japan in the 6th century; it was introduced in the year 538 or 552<sup id="cite_ref-Brown,_1993._p._455_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown,_1993._p._455-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> from the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Baekje" title="Baekje">Baekje</a> in <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown,_1993._p._455_256-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown,_1993._p._455-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> was first introduced into Japan by <a href="/wiki/Jesuit" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesuit">Jesuit</a> missions starting in 1549.<sup id="cite_ref-Higashibaba,_2002._p._1_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Higashibaba,_2002._p._1-257">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> Today, fewer than 1%<sup id="cite_ref-Mariko_Kato_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mariko_Kato-258">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mission_Network_News_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mission_Network_News-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Heide_Fehrenbach,_Uta_G._Poiger_2000_62_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heide_Fehrenbach,_Uta_G._Poiger_2000_62-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> to 2.3% are <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Japan" title="Christianity in Japan">Christians</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> most of them living in the western part of the country, where the missionaries\' activities were greatest during the 16th century. <a href="/wiki/Nagasaki_Prefecture" title="Nagasaki Prefecture">Nagasaki Prefecture</a> has the highest percentage of Christians: about 5.1% in 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> As of  2007<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Japan&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, there are 32,036 Christian priests and pastors in Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-BY65_255-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BY65-255">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> Throughout the latest century, some Western customs originally related to Christianity (including <a href="/wiki/Marriage_in_Japan" title="Marriage in Japan">Western style weddings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Valentine%27s_Day" title="Valentine&#39;s Day">Valentine\'s Day</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Christmas</a>) have become popular as secular customs among many Japanese.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Japan" title="Islam in Japan">Islam in Japan</a> is estimated to constitute, about 80\xe2\x80\x9390%, of foreign born migrants and their children, primarily from <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> Much of the ethnic Japanese Muslims are those who convert upon marrying immigrant Muslims.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup> The Pew Research Center estimated that there were 185,000 Muslims in Japan in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Other minority religions include <a href="/wiki/Hinduism_in_Japan" title="Hinduism in Japan">Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith" title="Bah\xc3\xa1&#39;\xc3\xad Faith">Bah\xc3\xa1\'\xc3\xad Faith</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Japan_Bah\xc3\xa1&#39;\xc3\xad_Network_267-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Japan_Bah\xc3\xa1&#39;\xc3\xad_Network-267">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup>  and since the mid-19th century numerous <a href="/wiki/Japanese_new_religions" title="Japanese new religions">new religious movements</a> have emerged in Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-Clarke_268-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke-268">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Japan" title="Languages of Japan">Languages of Japan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese language</a></div>\n<p>More than 99 percent of the population speaks Japanese as their first language.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_6-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Japanese is an <a href="/wiki/Agglutinative_language" title="Agglutinative language">agglutinative language</a> distinguished by a system of <a href="/wiki/Honorific_speech_in_Japanese" title="Honorific speech in Japanese">honorifics</a> reflecting the hierarchical nature of Japanese society, with verb forms and particular vocabulary indicating the relative status of speaker and listener. <a href="/wiki/Japanese_writing_system" title="Japanese writing system">Japanese writing</a> uses <a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">kanji</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_character" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese character">Chinese characters</a>) and two sets of <a href="/wiki/Kana" title="Kana">kana</a> (<a href="/wiki/Syllabary" title="Syllabary">syllabaries</a> based on <a href="/wiki/Cursive_script_(East_Asia)" title="Cursive script (East Asia)">cursive script</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radical_(Chinese_characters)" title="Radical (Chinese characters)">radical</a> of kanji), as well as the <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabic_numerals" title="Arabic numerals">Arabic numerals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Besides Japanese, the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyuan_languages" title="Ryukyuan languages">Ryukyuan languages</a> (<a href="/wiki/Amami_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Amami language">Amami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kunigami_language" title="Kunigami language">Kunigami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Okinawan_language" title="Okinawan language">Okinawan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miyako_language" title="Miyako language">Miyako</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yaeyama_language" title="Yaeyama language">Yaeyama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yonaguni_language" title="Yonaguni language">Yonaguni</a>), also part of the <a href="/wiki/Japonic_languages" title="Japonic languages">Japonic language family</a>, are spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands" title="Ryukyu Islands">Ryukyu Islands</a> chain. Few children learn these languages,<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup> but in recent years the local governments have sought to increase awareness of the traditional languages. The <a href="/wiki/Okinawan_Japanese" title="Okinawan Japanese">Okinawan Japanese</a> dialect is also spoken in the region. The <a href="/wiki/Ainu_language" title="Ainu language">Ainu language</a>, which has no proven relationship to Japanese or any other language, is <a href="/wiki/Moribund_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Moribund language">moribund</a>, with only a few elderly native speakers remaining in Hokkaido.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> Public and private schools generally require students to take Japanese language classes as well as <a href="/wiki/English_language_education_in_Japan" class="mw-redirect" title="English language education in Japan">English</a> language courses.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ItaGiappone_273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ItaGiappone-273">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Problems">Problems</span></h3>\n<p>The changes in demographic structure have created a number of social issues, particularly a potential decline in workforce population and increase in the cost of social security benefits such as the public pension plan.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup> A growing number of younger Japanese are not marrying or remain childless.<sup id="cite_ref-Ogawa_275-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ogawa-275">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> In 2011, Japan\'s population dropped for a fifth year, falling by 204,000 people to 126.24 million people. This was the greatest decline since at least 1947, when comparable figures were first compiled.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> This decline was made worse by the <a href="/wiki/2011_T%C5%8Dhoku_earthquake_and_tsunami" title="2011 T\xc5\x8dhoku earthquake and tsunami">March 2011 earthquake and tsunami</a>, which killed nearly 16,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Japan\'s population is expected to drop to 95 million by 2050;<sup id="cite_ref-handbook_249-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-handbook-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> demographers and government planners are currently in a heated debate over how to cope with this problem.<sup id="cite_ref-Ogawa_275-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ogawa-275">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Immigration_to_Japan" title="Immigration to Japan">Immigration</a> and birth incentives are sometimes suggested as a solution to provide younger workers to support the nation\'s ageing population.<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> Japan accepts an average flow of 9,500 new Japanese citizens by naturalization per year.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_High_Commissioner_for_Refugees" title="United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees">UNHCR</a>, in 2012 Japan accepted just 18 refugees for resettlement,<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup> while the United States took in 76,000.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Japan <a href="/wiki/Suicide_in_Japan" title="Suicide in Japan">suffers from a high suicide rate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_284-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-284">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Times_285-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Times-285">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> In 2009, the number of suicides exceeded 30,000 for the twelfth successive year.<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup> Suicide is the leading cause of death for people under 30.<sup id="cite_ref-ozawa-desilva_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ozawa-desilva-287">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Japan" title="Education in Japan">Education in Japan</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tokyo_University_Entrance_Exam_Results_6.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Tokyo_University_Entrance_Exam_Results_6.JPG/220px-Tokyo_University_Entrance_Exam_Results_6.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Tokyo_University_Entrance_Exam_Results_6.JPG/330px-Tokyo_University_Entrance_Exam_Results_6.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Tokyo_University_Entrance_Exam_Results_6.JPG/440px-Tokyo_University_Entrance_Exam_Results_6.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tokyo_University_Entrance_Exam_Results_6.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Students celebrating after the announcement of the results of the <a href="/wiki/Higher_education_in_Japan#University_entrance" title="Higher education in Japan">entrance examinations</a> to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Tokyo" title="University of Tokyo">University of Tokyo</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Primary schools, secondary schools and universities were <a href="/wiki/Education_in_the_Empire_of_Japan" title="Education in the Empire of Japan">introduced</a> in 1872 as a result of the Meiji Restoration.<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup> Since 1947, compulsory education in Japan comprises <a href="/wiki/Elementary_schools_in_Japan" title="Elementary schools in Japan">elementary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Secondary_education_in_Japan#Junior_high_school" title="Secondary education in Japan">junior high school</a>, which together last for nine years (from age 6 to age 15). Almost all children continue their education at a three-year <a href="/wiki/Secondary_education_in_Japan" title="Secondary education in Japan">senior high school</a>.\n</p><p>Japan\'s education system played a central part in the country\'s recovery and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_post-war_economic_miracle" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese post-war economic miracle">rapid economic growth</a> in the decades following the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. After World War II, the <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_Law_of_Education" title="Fundamental Law of Education">Fundamental Law of Education</a> and the School Education Law were enacted. The latter law defined the school system that would be in effect for many decades: six years of <a href="/wiki/Primary_education" title="Primary education">elementary school</a>, three years of <a href="/wiki/Middle_school" title="Middle school">junior high school</a>, three years of high school, and two or four years of university. Starting in April 2016, various schools began the academic year with elementary school and junior high school integrated into one nine-year compulsory schooling program, in hopes to mitigate <a href="/wiki/School_bullying" title="School bullying">bullying</a> and <a href="/wiki/Truancy" title="Truancy">truancy</a>; <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Education,_Culture,_Sports,_Science_and_Technology" title="Ministry of Education, Culture, Sports, Science and Technology">MEXT</a> plans for this approach to be adopted nationwide in the coming years.<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup> In Japan, having a strong educational background greatly improves the likelihood of finding a job and earning enough money to support oneself. Highly educated individuals are less affected by unemployment trends as higher levels of educational attainment make an individual more attractive in the workforce. The lifetime earnings also increase with each level of education attained. Furthermore, skills needed in the modern 21st century labor market are becoming more knowledge-based and strong aptitude in science and mathematics are more strong predictors of employment prospects in Japan\'s highly technological economy.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_290-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-290">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Japan is one of the top-performing <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> countries in reading literacy, maths and sciences with the average student scoring 540 and has one of the worlds highest-educated labor forces among OECD countries.<sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup> The Japanese populace is well educated and its society highly values education as a platform for social mobility and for gaining employment in the country\'s competitive high-tech economy. The country\'s large pool of highly educated and skilled individuals is largely responsible for ushering Japan\'s post-war <a href="/wiki/Japanese_post-war_economic_miracle" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese post-war economic miracle">economic growth</a>. Tertiary-educated adults in Japan, particularly graduates in sciences and engineering benefit economically and socially from their education and skills in the country\'s high tech economy.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_290-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-290">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup> Spending on education as a proportion of GDP is below the OECD average. Although expenditure per student is comparatively high in Japan, total expenditure relative to GDP remains small.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_290-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-290">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup> In 2015, Japan\'s public spending on education amounted to just 3.5 percent of its GDP, below the <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> average of 4.7%.<sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup> In 2014, the country ranked fourth for the percentage of 25- to 64-year-olds that have attained tertiary education with 48 percent. In addition, bachelor\'s degrees are held by 59 percent of Japanese aged 25\xe2\x80\x9334, the second most in the OECD after South Korea.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> As the Japanese economy is largely scientific and technological based, the labor market demands people who have achieved some form of higher education, particularly related to science and engineering in order to gain a competitive edge when searching for employment opportunities. About 75.9 percent of high school graduates attended a university, junior college, trade school, or other <a href="/wiki/Higher_education_in_Japan" title="Higher education in Japan">higher education</a> institution.<sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The two top-ranking universities in Japan are the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Tokyo" title="University of Tokyo">University of Tokyo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_University" title="Kyoto University">Kyoto University</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup> which have produced <a href="/wiki/List_of_Japanese_Nobel_laureates" title="List of Japanese Nobel laureates">16 Nobel Prize laureates</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Programme_for_International_Student_Assessment" title="Programme for International Student Assessment">Programme for International Student Assessment</a> coordinated by the OECD currently ranks the overall knowledge and skills of Japanese 15-year-olds as sixth best in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Japan" title="Health in Japan">Health in Japan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Health_care_system_in_Japan" title="Health care system in Japan">Health care system in Japan</a></div>\n<p>In Japan, health care is provided by national and local governments. Payment for personal medical services is offered through a universal health insurance system that provides relative equality of access, with fees set by a government committee. People without insurance through employers can participate in a national health insurance program administered by local governments. Since 1973, all elderly persons have been covered by government-sponsored insurance.<sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup> Patients are free to select the physicians or facilities of their choice.<sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Japan" title="Culture of Japan">Culture of Japan</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_popular_culture" title="Japanese popular culture">Japanese popular culture</a></div>\n<p>Japanese culture has evolved greatly from its origins. Contemporary culture combines influences from Asia, Europe and North America.<sup id="cite_ref-Haffner_299-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haffner-299">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup>  Traditional Japanese arts include <a href="/wiki/Japanese_handicrafts" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese handicrafts">crafts</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Japanese_pottery_and_porcelain" title="Japanese pottery and porcelain">ceramics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kimono" title="Kimono">textiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_lacquerware" title="Japanese lacquerware">lacquerware</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_sword" title="Japanese sword">swords</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_traditional_dolls" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese traditional dolls">dolls</a>; performances of <a href="/wiki/Bunraku" title="Bunraku">bunraku</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kabuki" title="Kabuki">kabuki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Noh" title="Noh">noh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_traditional_dance" title="Japanese traditional dance">dance</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rakugo" title="Rakugo">rakugo</a>; and other practices, the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_tea_ceremony" title="Japanese tea ceremony">tea ceremony</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ikebana" title="Ikebana">ikebana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_martial_arts" title="Japanese martial arts">martial arts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_calligraphy" title="Japanese calligraphy">calligraphy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Origami" title="Origami">origami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Onsen" title="Onsen">onsen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geisha" title="Geisha">Geisha</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Japanese_games" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Japanese games">games</a>. Japan has a developed system for the protection and promotion of both tangible and intangible <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Properties_of_Japan" class="mw-redirect" title="Cultural Properties of Japan">Cultural Properties</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Treasures_of_Japan" class="mw-redirect" title="National Treasures of Japan">National Treasures</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Sites_in_Japan" class="mw-redirect" title="World Heritage Sites in Japan">Nineteen sites</a> have been inscribed on the UNESCO World Heritage List, fifteen of which are of cultural significance.<sup id="cite_ref-unesco1_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unesco1-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_architecture" title="Japanese architecture">Japanese architecture</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kinkaku-ji_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Kinkaku-ji_01.jpg/220px-Kinkaku-ji_01.jpg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Kinkaku-ji_01.jpg/330px-Kinkaku-ji_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Kinkaku-ji_01.jpg/440px-Kinkaku-ji_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2590" data-file-height="1827" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kinkaku-ji_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kinkaku-ji" title="Kinkaku-ji">Kinkaku-ji</a> or "The Temple of the Golden Pavilion" in <a href="/wiki/Historic_Monuments_of_Ancient_Kyoto_(Kyoto,_Uji_and_Otsu_Cities)" title="Historic Monuments of Ancient Kyoto (Kyoto, Uji and Otsu Cities)">Kyoto</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Special_Places_of_Scenic_Beauty,_Special_Historic_Sites_and_Special_Natural_Monuments" title="List of Special Places of Scenic Beauty, Special Historic Sites and Special Natural Monuments">Special Historic Site, Special Place of Scenic Beauty</a> and a UNESCO World Heritage Site, whose torching by a monk in 1950 is the subject of a <a href="/wiki/The_Temple_of_the_Golden_Pavilion" title="The Temple of the Golden Pavilion">novel</a> by <a href="/wiki/Yukio_Mishima" title="Yukio Mishima">Mishima</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Japanese architecture is a combination between local and other influences. It has traditionally been typified by wooden structures, elevated slightly off the ground, with tiled or thatched roofs. Sliding doors (<i><a href="/wiki/Fusuma" title="Fusuma">fusuma</a></i>) were used in place of walls, allowing the internal configuration of a space to be customized for different occasions. People usually sat on cushions or otherwise on the floor, traditionally; chairs and high tables were not widely used until the 20th century. Since the 19th century, however, Japan has incorporated much of Western, <a href="/wiki/Modern_architecture" title="Modern architecture">modern</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Post-modern_architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Post-modern architecture">post-modern architecture</a> into construction and design, and is today a leader in cutting-edge architectural design and technology.\n</p><p>The introduction of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Japan" title="Buddhism in Japan">Buddhism</a> during the sixth century was a catalyst for large-scale <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_temples_in_Japan" title="Buddhist temples in Japan">temple</a> building using complicated techniques in wood. Influence from the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui</a> dynasties led to the foundation of the first permanent capital in <a href="/wiki/Nara,_Nara" title="Nara, Nara">Nara</a>. Its checkerboard street layout used the Chinese capital of <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang&#39;an">Chang\'an</a> as a template for its design. A gradual increase in the size of buildings led to standard units of measurement as well as refinements in layout and garden design. The introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_tea_ceremony" title="Japanese tea ceremony">tea ceremony</a> emphasised simplicity and modest design as a counterpoint to the excesses of the aristocracy.\n</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Restoration" title="Meiji Restoration">Meiji Restoration</a> of 1868 the history of Japanese architecture was radically changed by two important events. The first was the <a href="/wiki/Shinbutsu_bunri" title="Shinbutsu bunri">Kami and Buddhas Separation Act</a> of 1868, which formally separated Buddhism from <a href="/wiki/Shinto" title="Shinto">Shinto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_temples_in_Japan" title="Buddhist temples in Japan">Buddhist temples</a> from <a href="/wiki/Shinto_shrine" title="Shinto shrine">Shinto shrines</a>, breaking an association between the two which had lasted well over a thousand years.<sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Second, it was then that Japan underwent a period of intense <a href="/wiki/Westernization" title="Westernization">Westernization</a> in order to compete with other developed countries. Initially architects and styles from abroad were imported to Japan but gradually the country taught its own architects and began to express its own style. Architects returning from study with western architects introduced the <a href="/wiki/International_style_(architecture)" class="mw-redirect" title="International style (architecture)">International Style</a> of modernism into Japan. However, it was not until after the Second World War that Japanese architects made an impression on the international scene, firstly with the work of architects like <a href="/wiki/Kenz%C5%8D_Tange" title="Kenz\xc5\x8d Tange">Kenz\xc5\x8d Tange</a> and then with theoretical movements like <a href="/wiki/Metabolist_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Metabolist Movement">Metabolism</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_art" title="Japanese art">Japanese art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_garden" title="Japanese garden">Japanese garden</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_aesthetics" title="Japanese aesthetics">Japanese aesthetics</a></div>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;max-width:312px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:157px;max-width:157px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:102px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:HIRADO_PORCELAIN_CENSER_AND_COVER.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/HIRADO_PORCELAIN_CENSER_AND_COVER.JPG/155px-HIRADO_PORCELAIN_CENSER_AND_COVER.JPG" width="155" height="103" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/HIRADO_PORCELAIN_CENSER_AND_COVER.JPG/233px-HIRADO_PORCELAIN_CENSER_AND_COVER.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/HIRADO_PORCELAIN_CENSER_AND_COVER.JPG/310px-HIRADO_PORCELAIN_CENSER_AND_COVER.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3088" data-file-height="2056" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Hirado ware porcelain censers in the form of tiger and figurine with fan, brown and blue glazes.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:151px;max-width:151px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:102px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Great_Wave_off_Kanagawa.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/The_Great_Wave_off_Kanagawa.jpg/149px-The_Great_Wave_off_Kanagawa.jpg" width="149" height="103" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/The_Great_Wave_off_Kanagawa.jpg/224px-The_Great_Wave_off_Kanagawa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/The_Great_Wave_off_Kanagawa.jpg/298px-The_Great_Wave_off_Kanagawa.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4335" data-file-height="2990" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">19th-century <a href="/wiki/Ukiyo-e" title="Ukiyo-e">ukiyo-e</a> <a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing_in_Japan" title="Woodblock printing in Japan">woodblock print</a><i> <a href="/wiki/The_Great_Wave_off_Kanagawa" title="The Great Wave off Kanagawa">The Great Wave off Kanagawa</a></i></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ise_Grand_Shrine" title="Ise Grand Shrine">Shrines of Ise</a> have been celebrated as the prototype of Japanese architecture.<sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup> Largely of wood, <a href="/wiki/Minka" title="Minka">traditional housing</a> and many <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Buddhist_architecture" title="Japanese Buddhist architecture">temple buildings</a> see the use of <a href="/wiki/Tatami" title="Tatami">tatami</a> mats and <a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dji" title="Sh\xc5\x8dji">sliding doors</a> that break down the distinction between rooms and indoor and outdoor space.<sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Japanese_sculpture" title="Japanese sculpture">Japanese sculpture</a>, largely of wood, and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_painting" title="Japanese painting">Japanese painting</a> are among the oldest of the Japanese arts, with early figurative paintings dating back to at least 300 BC. The history of Japanese painting exhibits synthesis and competition between native <a href="/wiki/Japanese_aesthetics" title="Japanese aesthetics">Japanese aesthetics</a> and adaptation of imported ideas.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_304-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-304">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;max-width:312px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:165px;max-width:165px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:91px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Japanese-Calligraphy-art.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Japanese-Calligraphy-art.jpg/163px-Japanese-Calligraphy-art.jpg" width="163" height="92" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Japanese-Calligraphy-art.jpg/245px-Japanese-Calligraphy-art.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Japanese-Calligraphy-art.jpg/326px-Japanese-Calligraphy-art.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="1080" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Japanese_calligraphy" title="Japanese calligraphy">Japanese calligraphy</a> (shod\xc5\x8d)</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:143px;max-width:143px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:91px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Anime-Store-In-Akihabara.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Anime-Store-In-Akihabara.jpg/141px-Anime-Store-In-Akihabara.jpg" width="141" height="91" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Anime-Store-In-Akihabara.jpg/212px-Anime-Store-In-Akihabara.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Anime-Store-In-Akihabara.jpg/282px-Anime-Store-In-Akihabara.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1036" data-file-height="670" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">An <a href="/wiki/Anime" title="Anime">anime</a> store in <a href="/wiki/Akihabara" title="Akihabara">Akihabara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>The interaction between Japanese and European art has been significant: for example <a href="/wiki/Ukiyo-e" title="Ukiyo-e">ukiyo-e</a> prints, which began to be exported in the 19th century in the movement known as <a href="/wiki/Japonism" title="Japonism">Japonism</a>, had a significant influence on the development of modern art in the West, most notably on <a href="/wiki/Post-Impressionism" title="Post-Impressionism">post-Impressionism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_304-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-304">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup> Famous ukiyo-e artists include <a href="/wiki/Hokusai" title="Hokusai">Hokusai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hiroshige" title="Hiroshige">Hiroshige</a>.\n</p><p>Japanese comics, known as <a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">manga</a>, developed in the 20th century and have become popular worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rakuten_Kitazawa" class="mw-redirect" title="Rakuten Kitazawa">Rakuten Kitazawa</a> was first to use the word "manga" in the modern sense. Japanese-made <a href="/wiki/Video_game_console" title="Video game console">video game consoles</a> have been popular since the 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animation">Animation</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Anime" title="Anime">Anime</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">Manga</a></div>\n<p>Japanese animated films and television series, known as <a href="/wiki/Anime" title="Anime">anime</a> for short, were largely influenced by Japanese <a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">manga</a> comic books and have been extensively popular in the West. Japan is a world-renowned powerhouse of animation.<sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup>  Famous anime directors include <a href="/wiki/Hayao_Miyazaki" title="Hayao Miyazaki">Hayao Miyazaki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Osamu_Tezuka" title="Osamu Tezuka">Osamu Tezuka</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isao_Takahata" title="Isao Takahata">Isao Takahata</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cinema">Cinema</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Japan" title="Cinema of Japan">Cinema of Japan</a></div>\n<p>Japan has one of the oldest and largest film industries in the world; movies have been produced in Japan since 1897.<sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup> Three Japanese films (<i><a href="/wiki/Rashomon_(film)" class="mw-redirect" title="Rashomon (film)">Rashomon</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Seven_Samurai" title="Seven Samurai">Seven Samurai</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Story" title="Tokyo Story">Tokyo Story</a></i>) made the <i><a href="/wiki/Sight_%26_Sound" title="Sight &amp; Sound">Sight &amp; Sound</a>\'</i>s 2002 Critics and Directors Poll for the <a href="/wiki/Films_considered_the_greatest_ever" class="mw-redirect" title="Films considered the greatest ever">best films of all time</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ishir%C5%8D_Honda" title="Ishir\xc5\x8d Honda">Ishir\xc5\x8d Honda</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Godzilla_(1954_film)" title="Godzilla (1954 film)">Godzilla</a></i> became an international icon of Japan and spawned an entire subgenre of <i><a href="/wiki/Kaiju" title="Kaiju">kaiju</a></i> films, as well as the longest-running film franchise in history.  The most acclaimed Japanese film directors include <a href="/wiki/Akira_Kurosawa" title="Akira Kurosawa">Akira Kurosawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenji_Mizoguchi" title="Kenji Mizoguchi">Kenji Mizoguchi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yasujiro_Ozu" class="mw-redirect" title="Yasujiro Ozu">Yasujiro Ozu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shohei_Imamura" title="Shohei Imamura">Shohei Imamura</a>.  Japan has won the <a href="/wiki/Academy_Awards" title="Academy Awards">Academy Award</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Foreign_Language_Film" title="Academy Award for Best Foreign Language Film">Best Foreign Language Film</a> four times, more than any other Asian country.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etiquette">Etiquette</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Etiquette_in_Japan" title="Etiquette in Japan">Etiquette in Japan</a></div>\n<p>Throughout the millennia, since the prehistoric <a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dmon_period" title="J\xc5\x8dmon period">J\xc5\x8dmon period</a>, Japan developed a sophisticated culture and etiquette while absorbing influences from Asia, Europe, and North America.<sup id="cite_ref-Haffner_299-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haffner-299">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The code of <a href="/wiki/Etiquette_in_Japan" title="Etiquette in Japan">etiquette in Japan</a> governs the expectations of social behavior. They are considered very important in Japan. The etiquette varies greatly depending on one\'s status relative to the person in question. Some customs have changed overtime. These distinct cultural values make Japanese etiquette substantially different from western and other countries.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Honne_and_tatemae" title="Honne and tatemae">Honne and tatemae</a> (\xe6\x9c\xac\xe9\x9f\xb3\xe3\x81\xa8\xe5\xbb\xba\xe5\x89\x8d) contrasts a person\'s true feelings and desires and the behavior and opinions one displays in public.<sup id="cite_ref-310" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-310">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yamato-damashii" title="Yamato-damashii">Yamato-damashii</a> (\xe5\xa4\xa7\xe5\x92\x8c\xe9\xad\x82) is a term that refers to the cultural values and characteristics of the Japanese people. It originates from the <a href="/wiki/Heian_period" title="Heian period">Heian period</a> and describes the indigenous Japanese \'spirit\' or cultural values as opposed to cultural values of foreign nations. <a href="/wiki/Wa_(Japanese_culture)" title="Wa (Japanese culture)">Wa</a> (\xe5\x92\x8c) is a Japanese cultural concept that implies a peaceful unity and conformity within a social group, in which members prefer the continuation of a harmonious community over their personal interests.<sup id="cite_ref-Genzberger1994_311-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Genzberger1994-311">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bauman1994_312-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bauman1994-312">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Miai" title="Miai">Miai</a> (\xe8\xa6\x8b\xe5\x90\x88\xe3\x81\x84) is a Japanese traditional custom in which a woman and a man are introduced to each other to consider the possibility of marriage. It is a meeting opportunity with more serious considerations for the future as a process of courtship.<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ishin-denshin" title="Ishin-denshin">Ishin-denshin</a> (\xe4\xbb\xa5\xe5\xbf\x83\xe4\xbc\x9d\xe5\xbf\x83) is a Japanese idiom which denotes a form of interpersonal communication through unspoken mutual understanding.<sup id="cite_ref-Maynard-1993_314-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maynard-1993-314">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Isagiyosa" title="Isagiyosa">Isagiyosa</a> (\xe6\xbd\x94\xe3\x81\x95) is a virtue of the capability of accepting death with composure and equanimity. <a href="/wiki/Cherry_blossom" title="Cherry blossom">Cherry blossoms</a> are a symbol of isagiyosa in the sense of embracing the transience of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hansei" title="Hansei">Hansei</a> (\xe5\x8f\x8d\xe7\x9c\x81) is a central idea in Japanese culture, meaning to acknowledge one\'s own mistake and to pledge improvement.  <a href="/wiki/Kotodama" title="Kotodama">Kotodama</a> (\xe8\xa8\x80\xe9\x9c\x8a) refers to the Japanese belief that mystical powers dwell in words and names.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Japan" title="Music of Japan">Music of Japan</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KotoPlayer.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/KotoPlayer.jpg/220px-KotoPlayer.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/36/KotoPlayer.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="195" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KotoPlayer.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Masayo Ishigure playing 13-strings <a href="/wiki/Koto_(instrument)" title="Koto (instrument)">Koto</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Japanese music is eclectic and diverse. Many <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Japanese_musical_instruments" title="Traditional Japanese musical instruments">instruments</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Koto_(instrument)" title="Koto (instrument)">koto</a>, were introduced in the 9th and 10th centuries. The accompanied <a href="/wiki/Recitative" title="Recitative">recitative</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Noh" title="Noh">Noh</a> drama dates from the 14th century and the popular <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Japan#Folk_music" title="Music of Japan">folk music</a>, with the guitar-like <a href="/wiki/Shamisen" title="Shamisen">shamisen</a>, from the sixteenth.<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup> Western classical music, introduced in the late 19th century, now forms an integral part of Japanese culture. The imperial court ensemble <a href="/wiki/Gagaku" title="Gagaku">Gagaku</a> has influenced the work of some <a href="/wiki/20th-century_classical_music" title="20th-century classical music">modern</a> Western composers.<sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Notable classical composers from Japan include <a href="/wiki/Toru_Takemitsu" title="Toru Takemitsu">Toru Takemitsu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rentar%C5%8D_Taki" title="Rentar\xc5\x8d Taki">Rentar\xc5\x8d Taki</a>. Popular music in post-war Japan has been heavily influenced by American and European trends, which has led to the evolution of <a href="/wiki/J-pop" title="J-pop">J-pop</a>, or Japanese popular music.<sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Karaoke" title="Karaoke">Karaoke</a> is the most widely practiced cultural activity in Japan. A 1993 survey by the <a href="/wiki/Agency_for_Cultural_Affairs" title="Agency for Cultural Affairs">Cultural Affairs Agency</a> found that more Japanese had sung karaoke that year than had participated in traditional pursuits such as flower arranging (ikebana) or tea ceremonies.<sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_literature" title="Japanese literature">Japanese literature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_poetry" title="Japanese poetry">Japanese poetry</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg/220px-Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg" width="220" height="103" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg/330px-Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg/440px-Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="517" data-file-height="243" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>12th-century <a href="/wiki/Genji_Monogatari_Emaki" title="Genji Monogatari Emaki">illustrated handscroll</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_Genji" title="The Tale of Genji">The Tale of Genji</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/National_Treasures_of_Japan" class="mw-redirect" title="National Treasures of Japan">National Treasure</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The earliest works of Japanese literature include the <i><a href="/wiki/Kojiki" title="Kojiki">Kojiki</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Nihon_Shoki" title="Nihon Shoki">Nihon Shoki</a></i> chronicles and the <i><a href="/wiki/Man%27y%C5%8Dsh%C5%AB" title="Man&#39;y\xc5\x8dsh\xc5\xab">Man\'y\xc5\x8dsh\xc5\xab</a></i> <a href="/wiki/List_of_Japanese_poetry_anthologies" title="List of Japanese poetry anthologies">poetry anthology</a>, all from the 8th century and written in Chinese characters.<sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">&#91;319&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">&#91;320&#93;</a></sup> In the early Heian period, the system of <a href="/wiki/Phonogram_(linguistics)" title="Phonogram (linguistics)">phonograms</a> known as <i>kana</i> (<a href="/wiki/Hiragana" title="Hiragana">hiragana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">katakana</a>) was developed. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_the_Bamboo_Cutter" title="The Tale of the Bamboo Cutter">The Tale of the Bamboo Cutter</a></i> is considered the oldest Japanese narrative.<sup id="cite_ref-ispmsu_322-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ispmsu-322">&#91;321&#93;</a></sup> An account of Heian court life is given in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pillow_Book" title="The Pillow Book">The Pillow Book</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Sei_Sh%C5%8Dnagon" title="Sei Sh\xc5\x8dnagon">Sei Sh\xc5\x8dnagon</a>, while <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_Genji" title="The Tale of Genji">The Tale of Genji</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Murasaki_Shikibu" title="Murasaki Shikibu">Murasaki Shikibu</a> is often described as the world\'s first novel.<sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-324" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-324">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the Edo period, the <a href="/wiki/Ch%C5%8Dnin" title="Ch\xc5\x8dnin">ch\xc5\x8dnin</a> ("townspeople") overtook the samurai aristocracy as producers and consumers of literature. The popularity of the works of <a href="/wiki/Saikaku" class="mw-redirect" title="Saikaku">Saikaku</a>, for example, reveals this change in readership and authorship, while <a href="/wiki/Matsuo_Bash%C5%8D" title="Matsuo Bash\xc5\x8d">Bash\xc5\x8d</a> revivified the poetic tradition of the <a href="/wiki/Kokinsh%C5%AB" class="mw-redirect" title="Kokinsh\xc5\xab">Kokinsh\xc5\xab</a> with his <a href="/wiki/Haikai" title="Haikai">haikai</a> (<a href="/wiki/Haiku" title="Haiku">haiku</a>) and wrote the poetic travelogue <i><a href="/wiki/Oku_no_Hosomichi" title="Oku no Hosomichi">Oku no Hosomichi</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup> The Meiji era saw the decline of traditional literary forms as Japanese literature integrated Western influences. <a href="/wiki/Natsume_S%C5%8Dseki" title="Natsume S\xc5\x8dseki">Natsume S\xc5\x8dseki</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mori_%C5%8Cgai" title="Mori \xc5\x8cgai">Mori \xc5\x8cgai</a> were the first "modern" novelists of Japan, followed by <a href="/wiki/Ry%C5%ABnosuke_Akutagawa" title="Ry\xc5\xabnosuke Akutagawa">Ry\xc5\xabnosuke Akutagawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jun%27ichir%C5%8D_Tanizaki" title="Jun&#39;ichir\xc5\x8d Tanizaki">Jun\'ichir\xc5\x8d Tanizaki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yukio_Mishima" title="Yukio Mishima">Yukio Mishima</a> and, more recently, <a href="/wiki/Haruki_Murakami" title="Haruki Murakami">Haruki Murakami</a>. Japan has two <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel Prize-winning</a> authors\xe2\x80\x94<a href="/wiki/Yasunari_Kawabata" title="Yasunari Kawabata">Yasunari Kawabata</a> (1968) and <a href="/wiki/Kenzabur%C5%8D_%C5%8Ce" title="Kenzabur\xc5\x8d \xc5\x8ce">Kenzabur\xc5\x8d \xc5\x8ce</a> (1994).<sup id="cite_ref-ispmsu_322-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ispmsu-322">&#91;321&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_philosophy" title="Japanese philosophy">Japanese philosophy</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kitaro_Nishidain_in_Feb._1943.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Kitaro_Nishidain_in_Feb._1943.jpg/170px-Kitaro_Nishidain_in_Feb._1943.jpg" width="170" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Kitaro_Nishidain_in_Feb._1943.jpg/255px-Kitaro_Nishidain_in_Feb._1943.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Kitaro_Nishidain_in_Feb._1943.jpg/340px-Kitaro_Nishidain_in_Feb._1943.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="675" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kitaro_Nishidain_in_Feb._1943.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kitaro_Nishida" title="Kitaro Nishida">Kitaro Nishida</a>, one of the most notable Japanese philosophers</div></div></div>\n<p>Japanese philosophy has historically been a <a href="/wiki/Information_fusion" class="mw-redirect" title="Information fusion">fusion</a> of both foreign; particularly <a href="/wiki/Chinese_philosophy" title="Chinese philosophy">Chinese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_philosophy" title="Western philosophy">Western</a>, and uniquely Japanese elements. In its literary forms, Japanese philosophy began about fourteen centuries ago.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Archaeological_record" title="Archaeological record">Archaeological evidence</a> and early historical accounts suggest that Japan was originally an <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animistic culture</a>, which viewed the world as infused with <i>kami</i> (\xe7\xa5\x9e) or sacred presence as taught by <a href="/wiki/Shinto" title="Shinto">Shinto</a>, though it is not a philosophy as such, but has greatly influenced all other philosophies in their Japanese interpretations.<sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">&#91;325&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a> entered Japan from China around the 5th century A.D., as did <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_327-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-327">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup> Confucian ideals are still evident today in the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_society" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese society">Japanese concept of society</a> and the self, and in the organization of the <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">government</a> and the structure of <a href="/wiki/Society" title="Society">society</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_327-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-327">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup> Buddhism has profoundly impacted Japanese psychology, <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aesthetics" title="Aesthetics">aesthetics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-328" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-328">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Indigenous ideas of <a href="/wiki/Loyalty" title="Loyalty">loyalty</a> and honour have been held since the 16th century. Western philosophy has had its major impact in Japan only since the middle of the 19th century.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_cuisine" title="Japanese cuisine">Japanese cuisine</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Toshihana_tea_ceremony.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Toshihana_tea_ceremony.jpg/220px-Toshihana_tea_ceremony.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Toshihana_tea_ceremony.jpg/330px-Toshihana_tea_ceremony.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Toshihana_tea_ceremony.jpg/440px-Toshihana_tea_ceremony.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Toshihana_tea_ceremony.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Maiko" title="Maiko">Maiko</a> preparing teacups for tea ceremony</div></div></div>\n<p>Japanese cuisine is based on combining <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple foods</a>, typically <a href="/wiki/Japanese_rice" title="Japanese rice">Japanese rice</a> or <a href="/wiki/Japanese_noodles" title="Japanese noodles">noodles</a>, with a soup and <i><a href="/wiki/Okazu" title="Okazu">okazu</a></i>\xe2\x80\x94dishes made from <a href="/wiki/Fish_(food)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fish (food)">fish</a>, vegetable, <a href="/wiki/Tofu" title="Tofu">tofu</a> and the like\xe2\x80\x94to add flavor to the staple food.<sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup> In the early modern era ingredients such as red meats that had previously not been widely used in Japan were introduced. \n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Curry_rice_by_Hyougushi_in_Kyoto.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Curry_rice_by_Hyougushi_in_Kyoto.jpg/220px-Curry_rice_by_Hyougushi_in_Kyoto.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Curry_rice_by_Hyougushi_in_Kyoto.jpg/330px-Curry_rice_by_Hyougushi_in_Kyoto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Curry_rice_by_Hyougushi_in_Kyoto.jpg/440px-Curry_rice_by_Hyougushi_in_Kyoto.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Curry_rice_by_Hyougushi_in_Kyoto.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Japanese_curry" title="Japanese curry">Japanese curry</a> (\xe3\x82\xab\xe3\x83\xac\xe3\x83\xbc).</div></div></div>\n<p>Japanese cuisine is known for its emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Seasonal_food" title="Seasonal food">seasonality of food</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330">&#91;329&#93;</a></sup> quality of ingredients and presentation. Japanese cuisine offers a vast array of <a href="/wiki/Japanese_regional_cuisine" title="Japanese regional cuisine">regional specialties</a> that use traditional recipes and local ingredients. The phrase <i>ichij\xc5\xab-sansai</i><span style="font-weight: normal">&#32;(<span lang="ja">\xe4\xb8\x80\xe6\xb1\x81\xe4\xb8\x89\xe8\x8f\x9c</span>, "one soup, three sides")</span> refers to the makeup of a typical meal served, but has roots in classic <i><a href="/wiki/Kaiseki" title="Kaiseki">kaiseki</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Honzen-ry%C5%8Dri" title="Honzen-ry\xc5\x8dri">honzen</a></i>, and <i>y\xc5\xabsoku</i> cuisine. The term is also used to describe the first course served in standard <i>kaiseki</i> cuisine nowadays.<sup id="cite_ref-yomiuri_331-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yomiuri-331">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Japanese_curry" title="Japanese curry">Japanese curry</a> since its introduction to Japan from <a href="/wiki/British_India" class="mw-redirect" title="British India">British India</a> is so widely consumed that it can be called a <a href="/wiki/National_dish" title="National dish">national dish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Long2015_332-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Long2015-332">&#91;331&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Traditional Japanese sweets are known as <i>wagashi</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Goldstein2015_333-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldstein2015-333">&#91;332&#93;</a></sup> Ingredients such as <a href="/wiki/Red_bean_paste" title="Red bean paste">red bean paste</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mochi" title="Mochi">mochi</a> are used. More modern-day tastes includes <a href="/wiki/Green_tea_ice_cream" title="Green tea ice cream">green tea ice cream</a>, a very popular flavor.<sup id="cite_ref-FujitaStallings2008_334-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FujitaStallings2008-334">&#91;333&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kakig%C5%8Dri" title="Kakig\xc5\x8dri">Kakig\xc5\x8dri</a> is a shaved ice dessert flavored with syrup or condensed milk. It is usually sold and eaten at summer festivals. Popular Japanese beverages such as <a href="/wiki/Sake" title="Sake">sake</a>, which is a brewed rice beverage that, typically, contains 14%~17% <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">alcohol</a> and is made by multiple <a href="/wiki/Fermentation_(food)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fermentation (food)">fermentation</a> of rice.<sup id="cite_ref-Batt2014_335-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Batt2014-335">&#91;334&#93;</a></sup> Beer has been brewed in Japan since the late 1800s,<sup id="cite_ref-BoultonQuain2013_336-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BoultonQuain2013-336">&#91;335&#93;</a></sup> and is produced in many regions by companies including <a href="/wiki/Asahi_Breweries" title="Asahi Breweries">Asahi Breweries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kirin_Company" title="Kirin Company">Kirin Brewery</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sapporo_Brewery" title="Sapporo Brewery">Sapporo Brewery</a> \xe2\x80\x93 claiming to be the  oldest named  brand of beer in Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-Alexander2013_337-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alexander2013-337">&#91;336&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Holidays">Holidays</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_Japan" title="Public holidays in Japan">Public holidays in Japan</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg/220px-Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg" width="220" height="134" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg/330px-Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg/440px-Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg 2x" data-file-width="674" data-file-height="412" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Young ladies celebrate <a href="/wiki/Coming_of_Age_Day" title="Coming of Age Day">Coming of Age Day</a> (\xe6\x88\x90\xe4\xba\xba\xe3\x81\xae\xe6\x97\xa5 <i>Seijin no Hi</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Harajuku" title="Harajuku">Harajuku</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Officially, Japan has 16 national, government-recognized holidays. Public holidays in Japan are regulated by the Public Holiday Law (\xe5\x9b\xbd\xe6\xb0\x91\xe3\x81\xae\xe7\xa5\x9d\xe6\x97\xa5\xe3\x81\xab\xe9\x96\xa2\xe3\x81\x99\xe3\x82\x8b\xe6\xb3\x95\xe5\xbe\x8b <i>Kokumin no Shukujitsu ni Kansuru H\xc5\x8dritsu</i>) of 1948.<sup id="cite_ref-338" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-338">&#91;337&#93;</a></sup> Beginning in 2000, Japan implemented the <a href="/wiki/Happy_Monday_System" title="Happy Monday System">Happy Monday System</a>, which moved a number of national holidays to Monday in order to obtain a long weekend. In 2006, the country decided to add <a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dwa_Day" title="Sh\xc5\x8dwa Day">Sh\xc5\x8dwa Day</a>, a new national holiday, in place of <a href="/wiki/Greenery_Day" title="Greenery Day">Greenery Day</a> on April 29, and to move Greenery Day to May 4. These changes took effect in 2007. In 2014, the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Councillors" class="mw-redirect" title="House of Councillors">House of Councillors</a> decided to add Mountain Day<span style="font-weight: normal">&#32;(<span lang="ja">\xe5\xb1\xb1\xe3\x81\xae\xe6\x97\xa5</span><span style="display:none">,</span>&#32;<i>Yama no Hi</i>)</span> to the Japanese calendar on August 11, after lobbying by the Japanese Alpine Club. It is intended to coincide with the <a href="/wiki/Bon_Festival" title="Bon Festival">Bon Festival</a> vacation time, giving Japanese people an opportunity to appreciate Japan\'s mountains.<sup id="cite_ref-339" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-339">&#91;338&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-340" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-340">&#91;339&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The national holidays in Japan are <a href="/wiki/Japanese_New_Year" title="Japanese New Year">New Year\'s Day</a> on January 1, <a href="/wiki/Coming_of_Age_Day" title="Coming of Age Day">Coming of Age Day</a> on Second Monday of January, <a href="/wiki/National_Foundation_Day" title="National Foundation Day">National Foundation Day</a> on February 11, <a href="/wiki/Vernal_Equinox_Day" title="Vernal Equinox Day">Vernal Equinox Day</a> on March 20 or 21, <a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dwa_Day" title="Sh\xc5\x8dwa Day">Sh\xc5\x8dwa Day</a> on April 29, <a href="/wiki/Constitution_Memorial_Day" title="Constitution Memorial Day">Constitution Memorial Day</a> on May 3, <a href="/wiki/Greenery_Day" title="Greenery Day">Greenery Day</a> on May 4, <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_Day_(Japan)" title="Children&#39;s Day (Japan)">Children\'s Day</a> on May 5, <a href="/wiki/Marine_Day" title="Marine Day">Marine Day</a> on Third Monday of July, <a href="/wiki/Mountain_Day" title="Mountain Day">Mountain Day</a> on August 11, <a href="/wiki/Respect_for_the_Aged_Day" title="Respect for the Aged Day">Respect for the Aged Day</a> on Third Monday of September, <a href="/wiki/Autumnal_Equinox" class="mw-redirect" title="Autumnal Equinox">Autumnal Equinox</a> on September 23 or 24, <a href="/wiki/Health_and_Sports_Day" title="Health and Sports Day">Health and Sports Day</a> on Second Monday of October, <a href="/wiki/Culture_Day" title="Culture Day">Culture Day</a> on November 3, <a href="/wiki/Labour_Thanksgiving_Day" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour Thanksgiving Day">Labour Thanksgiving Day</a> on November 23, and <a href="/wiki/The_Emperor%27s_Birthday" title="The Emperor&#39;s Birthday">The Emperor\'s Birthday</a> on December 23.<sup id="cite_ref-341" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-341">&#91;340&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Festivals">Festivals</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_festivals" title="Japanese festivals">Japanese festivals</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:UenoParkHanami.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/UenoParkHanami.jpg/220px-UenoParkHanami.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/UenoParkHanami.jpg/330px-UenoParkHanami.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/UenoParkHanami.jpg/440px-UenoParkHanami.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1764" data-file-height="1198" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:UenoParkHanami.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Popular Japanese festival, <a href="/wiki/Hanami" title="Hanami">Hanami</a> celebration at <a href="/wiki/Ueno_Park" title="Ueno Park">Ueno Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a></div></div></div>\n<p>There are many festivals in Japan, which are called in Japanese as <i>matsuri</i> (\xe7\xa5\xad) which celebrate annually. There are no specific festival days for all of Japan; dates vary from area to area, and even within a specific area, but festival days do tend to cluster around traditional holidays such as <a href="/wiki/Setsubun" title="Setsubun">Setsubun</a> or <a href="/wiki/Obon" class="mw-redirect" title="Obon">Obon</a>. Festivals are often based around one <a href="/wiki/Ceremony" title="Ceremony">event</a>, with food stalls, <a href="/wiki/Entertainment" title="Entertainment">entertainment</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carnival" title="Carnival">carnival</a> <a href="/wiki/Game" title="Game">games</a> to keep people entertained. Its usually sponsored by a local <a href="/wiki/Shrine" title="Shrine">shrine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Temple" title="Temple">temple</a>, though they can be <a href="/wiki/Secular" class="mw-redirect" title="Secular">secular</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jnto.go.jp_342-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jnto.go.jp-342">&#91;341&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Notable festival often feature processions which may include elaborate floats. Preparation for these processions is usually organised at the level of neighborhoods, or <i>machi</i> (\xe7\x94\xba). Prior to these, the local <a href="/wiki/Kami" title="Kami">kami</a> may be ritually installed in <a href="/wiki/Mikoshi" title="Mikoshi">mikoshi</a> and paraded through the streets, such as <a href="/wiki/Gion" title="Gion">Gion</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kyoto" title="Kyoto">Kyoto</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hadaka_matsuri" class="mw-redirect" title="Hadaka matsuri">Hadaka</a> in <a href="/wiki/Okayama" title="Okayama">Okayama</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jnto.go.jp_342-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jnto.go.jp-342">&#91;341&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Japan" title="Sport in Japan">Sport in Japan</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sumo_ceremony.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Sumo_ceremony.jpg/220px-Sumo_ceremony.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Sumo_ceremony.jpg/330px-Sumo_ceremony.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Sumo_ceremony.jpg/440px-Sumo_ceremony.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="375" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sumo_ceremony.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sumo" title="Sumo">Sumo</a> wrestlers form around the referee during the ring-entering ceremony</div></div></div>\n<p>Traditionally, <a href="/wiki/Sumo" title="Sumo">sumo</a> is considered Japan\'s national sport.<sup id="cite_ref-343" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-343">&#91;342&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Japanese_martial_arts" title="Japanese martial arts">Japanese martial arts</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Judo" title="Judo">judo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karate" title="Karate">karate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kendo" title="Kendo">kendo</a> are also widely practiced and enjoyed by spectators in the country. After the Meiji Restoration, many Western sports were introduced in Japan and began to spread through the education system.<sup id="cite_ref-344" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-344">&#91;343&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Japan hosted the Summer Olympics in <a href="/wiki/1964_Summer_Olympics" title="1964 Summer Olympics">Tokyo in 1964</a> and the Winter Olympics in <a href="/wiki/1972_Winter_Olympics" title="1972 Winter Olympics">Sapporo in 1972</a> and <a href="/wiki/1998_Winter_Olympics" title="1998 Winter Olympics">Nagano in 1998</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-345" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-345">&#91;344&#93;</a></sup> Further, the country hosted the official <a href="/wiki/2006_Basketball_World_Championship" class="mw-redirect" title="2006 Basketball World Championship">2006 Basketball World Championship</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-346" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-346">&#91;345&#93;</a></sup> Tokyo will host the <a href="/wiki/2020_Summer_Olympics" title="2020 Summer Olympics">2020 Summer Olympics</a>, making Tokyo the first Asian city to host the Olympics twice.<sup id="cite_ref-347" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-347">&#91;346&#93;</a></sup> The country gained the hosting rights for the official <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_Volleyball_World_Championship" class="mw-redirect" title="Women&#39;s Volleyball World Championship">Women\'s Volleyball World Championship</a> on five occasions (<a href="/wiki/FIVB_Volleyball_Women%27s_World_Championship" title="FIVB Volleyball Women&#39;s World Championship">1967</a>, <a href="/wiki/FIVB_Volleyball_Women%27s_World_Championship" title="FIVB Volleyball Women&#39;s World Championship">1998</a>, <a href="/wiki/2006_FIVB_Volleyball_Women%27s_World_Championship" title="2006 FIVB Volleyball Women&#39;s World Championship">2006</a>, <a href="/wiki/2010_FIVB_Volleyball_Women%27s_World_Championship"
