title="2010 FIVB Volleyball Women&#39;s World Championship">2010</a>, <a href="/wiki/2018_FIVB_Volleyball_Women%27s_World_Championship" title="2018 FIVB Volleyball Women&#39;s World Championship">2018</a>), more than any other nation.<sup id="cite_ref-348" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-348">&#91;347&#93;</a></sup> Japan is the most successful Asian <a href="/wiki/Rugby_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Rugby Union">Rugby Union</a> country, winning the <a href="/wiki/Asian_Five_Nations" class="mw-redirect" title="Asian Five Nations">Asian Five Nations</a> a record 6 times and winning the newly formed <a href="/wiki/IRB_Pacific_Nations_Cup" class="mw-redirect" title="IRB Pacific Nations Cup">IRB Pacific Nations Cup</a> in 2011. Japan will host the 2019 IRB <a href="/wiki/Rugby_World_Cup" title="Rugby World Cup">Rugby World Cup</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-349" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-349">&#91;348&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Baseball_in_Japan" title="Baseball in Japan">Baseball</a> is currently the most popular spectator sport in the country. Japan\'s top professional league, now known as <a href="/wiki/Nippon_Professional_Baseball" title="Nippon Professional Baseball">Nippon Professional Baseball</a>, was established in 1936<sup id="cite_ref-350" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-350">&#91;349&#93;</a></sup> and is widely considered to be the highest level of professional baseball in the world outside of the North American <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major Leagues</a>. Since the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/J._League" class="mw-redirect" title="J. League">Japan Professional Football League</a> in 1992, association football has also gained a wide following.<sup id="cite_ref-351" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-351">&#91;350&#93;</a></sup> Japan was a venue of the <a href="/wiki/Intercontinental_Cup_(football)" title="Intercontinental Cup (football)">Intercontinental Cup</a> from 1981 to 2004 and co-hosted the <a href="/wiki/2002_FIFA_World_Cup" title="2002 FIFA World Cup">2002 FIFA World Cup</a> with South Korea.<sup id="cite_ref-352" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-352">&#91;351&#93;</a></sup> Japan has one of the most successful football teams in Asia, winning the <a href="/wiki/AFC_Asian_Cup" title="AFC Asian Cup">Asian Cup</a> four times.<sup id="cite_ref-353" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-353">&#91;352&#93;</a></sup> Also, Japan recently won the <a href="/wiki/2011_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="2011 FIFA Women&#39;s World Cup">FIFA Women\'s World Cup</a> in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-354" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-354">&#91;353&#93;</a></sup> Golf is also popular in Japan,<sup id="cite_ref-355" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-355">&#91;354&#93;</a></sup> as are forms of auto racing like the <a href="/wiki/Super_GT" title="Super GT">Super GT</a> series and <a href="/wiki/Formula_Nippon" class="mw-redirect" title="Formula Nippon">Formula Nippon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-356" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-356">&#91;355&#93;</a></sup> The country has produced one <a href="/wiki/National_Basketball_Association" title="National Basketball Association">NBA</a> player, <a href="/wiki/Yuta_Tabuse" title="Yuta Tabuse">Yuta Tabuse</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-consulteny_357-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-consulteny-357">&#91;356&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Video_Gaming">Video Gaming</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GitarFreaks_and_DrumMania_V_cabinets_and_player.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/GitarFreaks_and_DrumMania_V_cabinets_and_player.jpg/220px-GitarFreaks_and_DrumMania_V_cabinets_and_player.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/GitarFreaks_and_DrumMania_V_cabinets_and_player.jpg/330px-GitarFreaks_and_DrumMania_V_cabinets_and_player.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/GitarFreaks_and_DrumMania_V_cabinets_and_player.jpg/440px-GitarFreaks_and_DrumMania_V_cabinets_and_player.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GitarFreaks_and_DrumMania_V_cabinets_and_player.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A man playing a drumming <a href="/wiki/Arcade_game" title="Arcade game">arcade game</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Drummania" class="mw-redirect" title="Drummania">Drummania</a></i>) in <a href="/wiki/Tsukuba,_Ibaraki" title="Tsukuba, Ibaraki">Tsukuba, Ibaraki</a>, 2005.</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Video_gaming_in_Japan" title="Video gaming in Japan">Video gaming in Japan</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Video_gaming_in_Japan" title="Video gaming in Japan">Video gaming in Japan</a> is a major industry. Japan became a major exporter of video games during the <a href="/wiki/Golden_age_of_arcade_video_games" title="Golden age of arcade video games">golden age of arcade video games</a>, an era that began with the release of Taito\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Invaders" title="Space Invaders">Space Invaders</a></i> in 1978 and ended around the mid-1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-358" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-358">&#91;357&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-359" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-359">&#91;358&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-360" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-360">&#91;359&#93;</a></sup>. Japan became the most dominant country within the global video game industry, since the release of the <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_Entertainment_System" title="Nintendo Entertainment System">Nintendo Entertainment System</a> and the <a href="/wiki/History_of_video_game_consoles_(third_generation)" class="mw-redirect" title="History of video game consoles (third generation)">third generation of consoles</a>. Japan\'s dominance within the industry would continue for the next two decades, until <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Xbox_(console)" title="Xbox (console)">Xbox</a> consoles began challenging <a href="/wiki/Sony" title="Sony">Sony</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nintendo" title="Nintendo">Nintendo</a> in the 2000s.<sup id="cite_ref-361" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-361">&#91;360&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-362" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-362">&#91;361&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-363" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-363">&#91;362&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the Japanese gaming industry, arcades have remained popular through to the present day. As of 2009, out of Japan\'s $20 billion gaming market, $6 billion of that amount is generated from arcades, which represent the largest sector of the Japanese video game market, followed by home <a href="/wiki/Console_game" title="Console game">console games</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mobile_game" title="Mobile game">mobile games</a> at $3.5 billion and $2 billion, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-364" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-364">&#91;363&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the present day, Japan is the world\'s largest market for <a href="/wiki/Mobile_game" title="Mobile game">mobile games</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-365" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-365">&#91;364&#93;</a></sup> The country\'s traditional console gaming market itself is today largely dominated by <a href="/wiki/Handheld_game_console" title="Handheld game console">handheld game consoles</a> rather than <a href="/wiki/Home_console" class="mw-redirect" title="Home console">home consoles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-366" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-366">&#91;365&#93;</a></sup> In 2014, Japan\'s consumer video game market grossed $9.6 billion, with $5.8 billion coming from mobile gaming.<sup id="cite_ref-367" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-367">&#91;366&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Media_of_Japan" title="Media of Japan">Media of Japan</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fuji_TV_headquarters_and_Aqua_City_Odaiba_-_2006-05-03_edit.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Fuji_TV_headquarters_and_Aqua_City_Odaiba_-_2006-05-03_edit.jpg/220px-Fuji_TV_headquarters_and_Aqua_City_Odaiba_-_2006-05-03_edit.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Fuji_TV_headquarters_and_Aqua_City_Odaiba_-_2006-05-03_edit.jpg/330px-Fuji_TV_headquarters_and_Aqua_City_Odaiba_-_2006-05-03_edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Fuji_TV_headquarters_and_Aqua_City_Odaiba_-_2006-05-03_edit.jpg/440px-Fuji_TV_headquarters_and_Aqua_City_Odaiba_-_2006-05-03_edit.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2013" data-file-height="1344" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fuji_TV_headquarters_and_Aqua_City_Odaiba_-_2006-05-03_edit.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Fuji_TV" title="Fuji TV">Fuji TV</a> headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">Television</a> and <a href="/wiki/Newspaper" title="Newspaper">newspapers</a> take an important role in Japanese mass media, though <a href="/wiki/Radio" title="Radio">radio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magazine" title="Magazine">magazines</a> also take a part.<sup id="cite_ref-nhk.or.jp_368-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nhk.or.jp-368">&#91;367&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-japanesestudies.org.uk_369-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-japanesestudies.org.uk-369">&#91;368&#93;</a></sup> For a long time, newspapers were regarded as the most influential information medium in Japan, although audience attitudes towards television changed with the emergence of commercial news broadcasting in the mid-1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-nhk.or.jp_368-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nhk.or.jp-368">&#91;367&#93;</a></sup> Over the last decade, television has clearly come to surpass newspapers as Japan\'s main information and entertainment medium.<sup id="cite_ref-pressnet.or.jp_370-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pressnet.or.jp-370">&#91;369&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There are 6 nationwide television networks: <a href="/wiki/NHK" title="NHK">NHK</a> (<a href="/wiki/Public_broadcasting" title="Public broadcasting">public broadcasting</a>), <a href="/wiki/Nippon_Television" class="mw-redirect" title="Nippon Television">Nippon Television</a> (NTV), <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Broadcasting_System" title="Tokyo Broadcasting System">Tokyo Broadcasting System</a> (TBS), <a href="/wiki/Fuji_Network_System" title="Fuji Network System">Fuji Network System</a> (FNS), <a href="/wiki/TV_Asahi" title="TV Asahi">TV Asahi</a> (EX) and <a href="/wiki/TV_Tokyo_Network" class="mw-redirect" title="TV Tokyo Network">TV Tokyo Network</a> (TXN).<sup id="cite_ref-japanesestudies.org.uk_369-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-japanesestudies.org.uk-369">&#91;368&#93;</a></sup> For the most part, television networks were established based on capital investments by existing radio networks. <a href="/wiki/Japanese_variety_show" title="Japanese variety show">Variety shows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_television_drama" title="Japanese television drama">serial dramas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/News" title="News">news</a> constitute a large percentage of Japanese television show. According to the 2015 NHK survey on television viewing in Japan, 79 percent of Japanese watch television every day. The average daily duration of television viewing was three hours.<sup id="cite_ref-371" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-371">&#91;370&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Japanese readers have a choice of approximately 120 daily newspapers with a total of 50 million copies of set paper with an average subscription rate of 1.13 newspapers per household.<sup id="cite_ref-372" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-372">&#91;371&#93;</a></sup> The main newspaper\'s publishers are <a href="/wiki/Yomiuri_Shimbun" title="Yomiuri Shimbun">Yomiuri Shimbun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asahi_Shimbun" title="Asahi Shimbun">Asahi Shimbun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mainichi_Shimbun" title="Mainichi Shimbun">Mainichi Shimbun</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Nikkei" title="The Nikkei">Nikkei Shimbun</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sankei_Shimbun" title="Sankei Shimbun">Sankei Shimbun</a>. According to a survey conducted by the Japanese Newspaper Association in June 1999, 85.4 per cent of men and 75 per cent of women read a newspaper every day. Average daily reading times vary with 27.7 minutes on weekdays and 31.7 minutes on holidays and Sunday.<sup id="cite_ref-pressnet.or.jp_370-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pressnet.or.jp-370">&#91;369&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>\n<div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal Retrieved <span class="nowrap">January 29,</span> 2017</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=National+Symbols&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.japan-guide.com%2Fe%2Fe2270.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJapan" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.metro.tokyo.jp/ENGLISH/ABOUT/HISTORY/history01.htm">"History of Tokyo"</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">January 29,</span> 2017</span>. <q>The Edo Period lasted for nearly 260 years until the Meiji Restoration in 1868, when the Tokugawa Shogunate ended and imperial rule was restored. The Emperor moved to Edo, which was renamed Tokyo.
